RATING: 6
Many nice things to say about this game - and can only agree that the components are marvelous and add to the enjoyment. It feels different from other worker placement games. For example, I very much enjoy the rolling to determine if you lose workers or not. It obviously curtails getting too many. And it adds to pulling off workers - do you pull them all and risk a large number or pull a few and ensure continued use? The beginning recruits really determine much of how you play - and getting different allegiances is (apparently) important. There however is luck in two significant ways: rolling doubles (which allows extra turns) and drawing artifacts (as two matching are as good as any three) - either may leave a feeling of unfairness. It can also become clear concerning the winner well before the game leaves the table - as you can at best only place 1 star on the board per turn. Still, an enjoyable game that should see a few more plays yet.

RATING: N/A
Abstract:
An innovative worker placement game that introduces worker bumping: players cannot block certain spots with their workers. A bumped worker is returned with a reward instead. The game also uses different (intelligent) types of worker.

Thoughts: 
I really like the unusual, dystopian theme. I also like different types of worker. I do not like how dice rolling is implemented in this game - I think it does not matter over the course of the game, however, it still feels luck-prone.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
WOW!!! Now, the details:

The second-best worker-placement game I have ever played after Carson City, and it gives Carson City a run for the money. It feels unlike any other worker-placement game out there, even Alien Frontiers, with which it shares the "dice as workers" mechanic. Remarkably well-themed, and I've spent a lot of time thinking about how it could be re-themed, and haven't come up with a perfect scenario: too many mechanics would have to be changed for a re-theme. 

Unlike so many other worker-placement games, Euphoria has no "hey! you took my spot!" feature. Workers can easily be booted out, often with delicious consequences. Although there are occasionally tactical elements (especially with the "when to boot" decision), Euphoria is overwhelmingly a strategic game, and a pretty scary one at that! Remember: it pays to be stupid! Knowledge equals unhappiness. 

There is a strong element of uncertainty which prevents players from ever feeling confident about their chosen strategy. And with such a large number of recruits and markets absent from any given game of Euphoria, replayability is very much a sure thing.

My only complaint is the rulebook. Everything is there and it's all quite clear; but a lack of context leaves one saying, "OK: I now understand how this works. But why is it important?" A more thematic explanation, I think, might have made it better.

My initial rating is a 9; but I think it's going to go up!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Will be playing this again.

RATING: 7
There is much to like in this game including excellent art, components, and an original theme.  The theme felt fresh and interesting when explained.  Upon first examining the board, and looking over the components, I actually felt a strong desire to really dive into the theme and experience a dystopia built from the hands and minds of the players.  

Yet, perhaps I was setting my sights and feelings too high.  The building of the dystopia in the game fell a little flat for me thematically.  I felt more like I was going through the same old euro gaming motions, creating a distance between myself and the theme.  

On the other hand, the standard euro gaming motions were certainly nicely designed and illustrated, so one could do a lot worse than this game if you are in the mood for a dice-based worker placement game.  And I suppose if the mechanics and the theme were tightened up too much, actual dystopic feelings might arise, and do we really want to spend game time experiencing dystopic feelings?!  Maybe not after all.  Better to have fun.

RATING: 7.5
This worker placement game deserves to be much higher than 242 on the BGG list. At least top 100. It is innovative and easy to play. Yes, you look at that board and think "Wow! That looks busy!" But it isn't. The game play is relatively simple but with enough strategy that you cannot just blunder through it and hope to win. The worker placement is different in that you can bump other people's workers and take that spot, though in doing so you give them back a worker to place on their turn. Euphoria is filled with other neat mechanisms like the morale & intelligence tracks and being able to gain allegiances with factions on the board . This is a great game and everyone ought to check it out so it can be higher as it deserves.

RATING: 9
One of my all time favorite games. The use of recruit cards that have special powers add an extreme amount of replayability. Each game you have a combination of two, and they greatly influence your strategy so there is no dominant way to win the game. Your path to victory will be different each game. I also really love how you play until someone wins outright, not for a fixed period and then count up points. This adds so much more tension to the game. Add these great concepts to a very strong dice-worker placement foundation and you get one hell of a great game.

RATING: 7
solid worker placement game with an interesting dystopia theme. Artwork is family friendly and vibrant, not 100% my style but it works. The game does a good job of being relatively simple with enough complexity to make play enjoyable once you figure out the legends and pathways to resources.

Great execution of the workers as dice mechanic, using rolls to check your workers knowledge against their willingness to keep working for you. Also allows players to bump each other as a way to access desirable spaces at the cost of saving a foe from having to pay to recall their workers.

Recruit card abilities or powers seem to range from decent/ok to pretty useless so some combinations of faction abilities could seem op. But most of the time their powers or abilities could use a boost.

Still a fun worker placement game that provides variety and enjoyment for 2-6 players. I don't own euphoria and will likely play it enough with our local group to avoid purchasing it but I wouldn't be mad about having it in my collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Euphoria is part classic worker placement euro, part innovative design.

The classic part is you place workers to obtain resources, convert these in better resources (or more workers), then into a star. After ten stars, you win.

The innovative part lies mainly in theme and its integration in gameplay: the backstory has some kind of sense, and many of the actions are integrated inside the theme and make it shine. The knowledge that brings workers to quit is quite ingenious, even if it brings a heavy dose of luck inside the game: higher rolls make you lose a worker, doubles or triples are insanely good...

Another (unusual for a eurogame) source of potential luck lies in the market tiles distribution: some are more "fit" for particular areas…

The third and most problematic source of luck is the recruit choice, done contemporarily at the beginning of the game: since the advancement in the faction tracks obviously favors players with the same faction recruits, being alone with a recruit of a faction seems very penalizing.

Anyway, Euphoria is a decent game, and it would probably have been great with a proper refined development.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
I had high expectations for this one. But it fell rather flat after my first play.
Don't get me wrong, it's a solid game, but there are just a few things/mechanisms that I don't like that much:
* Too many cards/tiles that change rules/give special effects (I don't like the aspect of keeping track of too many of those)
* The blind card draw and faction association - if you're the only one who sided with a faction you have bet on the losing horse (which you couldn't know)
* In our 6-player game we had 2 people who got locked out of doing anything with green faction (arguably the late game faction) - this might be a very unfortunate constellation of monuments but it left a sour taste

However, there are positive things as well:
* I liked the dice as workers with intelligence - the smarter your workers (sum of all pips), the more likely one runs off (you lose the die)
* Some actions can't be blocked, instead your worker returns to your hand if another person uses the action
* Components are good and the design is appealing (although maybe a bit overwhelming/confusing at times)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
First Play: components are of an awesome quality. The bar has been raised high here. However its downhill after that. I admit that dice rolling games rarely score highly for me but its a bad sign where the dice are the least offensive aspect of a game. Here the randomness of the cards utterly unbalances applied skill. The initial selection of 'agents' will be massively assisted if another player picks the same as you.   The artefacts can fall in your lap giving you doubles or exactly what you want....or they don't. Need a specific artefact for your card or to let you build in a construction? You'll need luck to do this efficiently, likewise the turn of the constructions result in some harsh or soft pay back for the non contributor. There is no way to tell until its flipped. The differences will make a huge effect on your game but you have no control over them or means to predict or check what they might be. Throughout there are random moments where gambles pay off or fail, not to mention that you need to roll low numbers when the dice are involved. 

I really don't care too much about theme and its just as well with Euphoria. I didn't understand the back story and the game play is just a series of worker placements in abstract zones on the board, gaining abstract materials and artefacts with pictures of teddy bears and baseball bats (???) and placing even more abstract stars for no tangible reason apart from the fact that placing 10 stars will win you the game. Perhaps this strangeness is the point, whatever the point, it was lost on me.

If you like this sort of luck fuelled experience then go for it, but I never want to play this again. 

4/10 rating because of the great bits.

RATING: 3
You won’t fool me again, Euphoria!

But all the fault is mine, I guess: I should have known better. I had to expect that your beautiful cover art and appealing theme were made just to lure new players into your tempting coils and have nothing to do with the real gameplay!
And so it was indeed: as soon as I joined it, Euphoria turned out as an uninspired downer that at first makes you regret joining it and then constantly revels in burdening you.

Anyhow, I was surprised by the level of luck involved, for an euro: you really can win or lose a game even before the real game starts, as almost everything hangs on the specialists you choose at the start and the faction they belong to. As a matter of fact, sharing the same faction as another player greatly improves the involved players’ chances at success.
To say nothing of the dice rolling, which is both Euphoria’s best feature (as it adds a hint of randomness) and worst rule (as it bullies the players who unlocked their fourth die) at the same time.

By the way: the cog-dice are such a beauty!

RATING: 8
2013. kickstarter. dice, wkr pl, set coll, hand mgt. similar to Alien Frontiers. An interesting economic and race game involving set collection and pick-up-and-deliver.  The goal is to be the first player to build all 10 of your achievement tokens.  There is a lot of luck in die rolls and artifact card draws, but you can mitigate some of the luck with skillful play of your workers on certain action spaces of the board.  I like that you can bump other players regardless of the value of your die... but bumping shouldn't be taken lightly since it essentially gives the bumped player a free action because he would normally have to pay to get his worker (die) back from the board.

I dislike that random draws of artifact cards have such a big impact on the game and it's not easy/cheap to obtain artifact cards (and that you often need one or two specific artifacts but there are ~12 different artifacts repeated in the artifact deck).  Maybe I need to build a better engine and cycle through those artifact cards or use them in different ways.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The single best worker placement game I've played. This game solved all but one of my complaints about worker placement games without losing any of their appeal.

Dice fly around constantly affecting the game but not in the way most games use them so that high rolls aren't necessarily better than low ones, just different. Players heavily interact with one another preventing the game from feeling mostly like a solo game that happens to have 4 other people competing for points at the end. There isn't even a single "must-have" spot on the board that needs to be limited to a single user and even if someone is squatting on a space you need, you can always bump them from your space although it helps them slightly. The game scoring is set up such that everyone is almost always within 2 points of finishing by the end of the game so even though those two points might take you as much as 6 turns, people don't usually *feel* like they got stomped at the end.

The only complaints I have about it is that when using 3 players, the recruits unbalance the game significantly because there is a good chance that one player will be trying to build up a faction all alone, which is a major hit to their productivity even if the recruit powers themselves are usually pretty minor and well balanced. The game also gets longer as you add more players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
What a charming, delightful game! This just might be our new Ticket to Ride: one of the few games that we often want to play again immediately after finishing. Not only is the theme incredibly original and inventive, but it is thoughtfully drawn and incredibly detailed, from the unique role cards to the hilarious construction markets and ethical dilemma cards. Each action you take, each space on the board absolutely oozes theme, which elevates it from being just another dice-placement game to a truly remarkable and memorable gaming experience.

The mechanics are also just as innovative: trying to "game" the dice placement so that you don't retrieve all your workers at once, all the while trying not to bump your opponents' workers back to them, is an absolutely awesome new idea for worker placement. I don't mind the luck element of the dice—though high rolls can cripple a player's work force and doubles do give possibly too much of a benefit—but there is somewhat of a balance issue if more than one person chooses the same faction in their opening hands: by "cooperating" early on, two players can easily gain the benefits of their shared factions and really shoot ahead of the others. Otherwise, fantastic!

RATING: 4
There are some really interesting aspects to this game that are unfortunately married to an uninspired resource management game with some unmitigateble luck (although the variants do address this luck a bit).

The dice are the interesting part (even though designwise they are difficult to read and cause for some confusion). Specifically the whole rolling when you receive them, roll too high and you lose them, paired with the intelligence mechanic, the ability to increase your dice pool for a hefty cost, the same intelligence rule gives more actions (but encourages extra workers even though ore workers risks losing them), and the bumpable (dotted lines), semi-permanent (large solid boxes), and slightly more permanent (market builders) spots on the board. Especially the bumpables where opponents might be encouraged to do something not a hundred percent critical right now if it means you'll lose a worker permanently.

However, then we come to the uninspired resource conversion game. Go here, get that, then go there and turn that into those, where three of the go here get that places are identical in all save for what they get you and who benefits long term (which I'll address more in the unfortunate luck section). 

Why is the game a resource conversion game? I can't help but think that there must have been something interesting, or at least, less repetitive than this that could have been done to showcase the whole rolling of dice mechanic. Maybe even something thematic. I've read dystopic fiction quite a bit (took a class on it once, even), never encountered a whole lot on resource generation and transformation or digging tunnels to produce resources, or any of that.

Finally we come to the unfortunate luck, the faction cards and the markets.

A huge help to winning is getting dealt the great faction cards as opposed to the near useless ones, and getting them in the same faction as those chosen by someone else. Neither of which you have any control over. But, if you luck out like that, you are going to have better resource production than the players who didn't get lucky, a special power that is more powerful that theirs (so, very tilted asymmetrical starting positions), and you'll get to place out your stars faster than them (because your influence column will complete first with more players pushing it along helping it complete).

And finally the markets. Being placed face down we never have any knowledge of what we are building, so why choose one over another? It's pure random and tends to cause further problems with the faction dominance because the larger factions will simply build up the markets associated with them and others will have to join in at helping the already dominant faction if they want to get in on those markets.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game is quite easy, and turns should only take a few seconds. And yet it is incredibly hard to teach and wrap your head around. I wonder if a player guide or a different graphic design might help. 

Maybe I’ll make a reference sheet that says “Need cards? Go tunneling or to Icarus. Need water? Try ___. Have extra stone? Use it for ___.” Etc. The only problem with this game is that new players don’t really have direction, don’t really know what to do next. With some help, I think this could be a bit more obvious.

RATING: 4
A very repetitive and luck-filled resource conversion game. Get x resource. Use x to get y. Use y to get z etc. There are a lot of luck elements that are annoying as well. Losing a die hurts a lot, so terrible rolls can ruin your game. Plus, the power of the special cards vary widely and if other people have your color, it is very advantageous for multiple people to be working together, and you're screwed if you're stuck by yourself. There are also huge luck elements with the cards. Getting pairs is absurdly powerful, for no particular reason, and when tiles are filpped, having a particular card can essentially guarantee you a star. 

The worker placement is weak. You are never precluded from going on any space. You just bump the previous guy out. That does create some interesting timing issues, but its very much a solitaire experience. 

The thing barely took an hour with 5 though and it has some charms so its not torture to play (Update after 2nd play: - yeah, it kinda is). Nothing special though. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
First of all, I love that this is a worker placement game that has a racing element in it – the first player to get 10 stars immediately wins! That’s always refreshing compared to many eurogames in which you tally up your points at the end. However, that’s probably the only good thing about the game… First of all, it’s just not that interesting to play; the mechanics are pretty basic and the variety is, imho, low. The game tries to camouflage it by giving you rather boring options, and even though the bumping is a good idea, it also means that you cannot really block any space. And if you bump a player, that player then gets an extra turn in which he doesn’t have to retrieve his workers, so your move actually benefits him… 

But by far the worst thing about the game is the randomness…! It oozes of it! It’s random which factories show up and if you have the cards which are needed for those and are following a strategy which suits those. It’s random which cards you draw; 2 of a kind is almost always better than 3 different. And speaking of those cards, the special starting cards are random too and certainly not balanced (which I’m not the only one claiming). And there’s of course the randomness concerning the dice; 2 of a kind is almost always better because you get a double turn… Oh, and I also twice rolled 15 and once 16 (and I only had 1 single brain!) with just 3 dice, while an opponent rolled much lower (which is good) with 4 dice… This essentially meant that I lost a lot of workers due to poor rolls – and there was no way to mitigate this; I had even played as safe as possible as I only had 1 brain… All of this randomness bugs me in a game which claims to be a medium eurogame. It honestly felt like it was random who won…

I’ve played it with 3 and 6, and none of those numbers were particularly good – but even though I would expect 4 to be the best number, the game is still bad. So, yes, my rating is actually generous and should probably be decreased, but I’m willing to give it one final shot.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I really enjoy worker placement games, but it can be tough to inject the genre with anything new.  Yet this game has done that quite nicely, and I can't wait to play it again.

In Euphoria you're trying to have your workers contribute to a dystopian society without becoming too aware of what's going on around them (solution: don't keep them together if you can help it).  You're gathering resources and trading them in for what you need to earn points.  But one key mechanism which really works are the building of markets, which require some group collaboration.  Whomever doesn't contribute faces a stiff penalty, so negotiation becomes a necessary evil.

Meanwhile most actions you take will advance the power of 1 of 4 factions, and as those factions grow, so do their bonuses, and you might get to reveal a helper card for an even bigger bonus.  With that there's a little bit of a hidden role aspect (as in which faction you want to "win").

Complicated rules, but once gameplay gets up to speed it works really well.  Beautiful production values.  Definitely worth checking out if you'd like a twist on your typical Eurogame.

RATING: 5
This game is gorgeous, clearly a labor of love, and has some really interesting concepts / systems / rules. (Some favorites: the "avoid a penalty" market dynamic; the secondary effects on farm harvesting; the effects of recruits on some action spaces.)

But ultimately, the role (roll?) of chance is both too prominent and too powerful for my tastes - mostly in losing workers due to too much knowledge (awesome concept, though) and in "go again on doubles", with recruit variability also seeming a bit rough.

Some have said that there's no interaction because workers don't block each other. This is untrue; there's plenty of interaction elsewhere (market-building, faction-status, mining, and farm rewards), as well as in the worker-placement piece - it's just that anticipating where someone else wants to go gives you a benefit (free worker reclaim) instead of giving them a penalty (can't take action).

RATING: 7.5
A dice-rolling game with fantastic components. Interestingly, the dice are most often used simply as workers, with no regard to the value shown on the die. (So, there's less luck than you might expect from a dice-centric game.)

Given the theme, I was expecting some more serious "ethical dilemma" types of choices and deeper gameplay, but the game is actually a fairly straightforward grab of resources (including cards) and exchanging them for points. Like Catan, it's a race to 10 points, so it shares the good and the bad of that mechanic.

(There are also recruit cards which provide some special powers and asymmetry and a little punishment in the game if you don't help build and open certain areas.)

Although I was expecting something a little different, it's definitely a good game for what it sets out to be.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Two plays in, this is a minor hit. It's imposing at first, but after a few turns everything flows smoothly. It's more tactical than strategic -- rather than picking a strategy and sticking to it, you get to try a number of different angles over the course of the game. Couple that with the reasonable play time, and Euphoria doesn't outstay its welcome.

It's a worker-placement game where your workers don't get locked out of most spaces. Instead, some spaces allow you to bump another player's worker (giving that player the advantage of retrieving the worker without taking a retrieval turn), while others allow you join other players' workers (with the result affected by all the workers in the space). These elements cleverly remove the frustration factor of many other WP games.

It's a little on the dry side to be sure, but that's partially mitigated by the truly wonderful components, especially the Kickstarter "realistic" resource tokens. If it weren't for the physical awesomeness, I might rate this a half point lower.


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Another mix of dice and worker placement. It's interesting if a bit chaotic, as much of your strategy will hinge on what the other players do, what you draw and, of course, what you roll. Still, it seems like there's some strategy and meaningful decisions, so it's still interesting. The characters that provide bonuses and the overall theme are a nice touch.

That said, it seems the trend now among eurogames, in an effort to stay interesting, is an ever increasing set of complex relationships between resources and goals. I feel like eurogame design has hit a wall to some degree, and this game is right up against it.

RATING: 7
Review: http://www.goingcardboard.ro/euphoria-standarde-ridicate-intr-un-euro-cu-pretentii/
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The mechanism that makes Euphoria stand out from other worker placement games is the ability to bump workers --- one's own or another player's --- from their placements. The bumpee (by getting bumped by his own workers or by anticipating the placements of other players) can employ this feature to great strategic effect to minimize the number of turns that he must use to retrieve workers. Although the bump is less useful to the bumper, he still may want to do it to force the bumpee into a knowledge check that may result in the loss of a worker.

The Catan-like race to ten also is a welcome departure from the usual goal of accumulating victory points.

RATING: 7.5
[2014-03-21 Rating: n.a. -> 7.5]
Another rating after only one game but since I don't own the game, I'm not sure whether I'll get another chance to play it. So this is more of an impression than a solid opinion that I've formed after a lot plays.

All in all, Euphoria is a game I really enjoyed playing. It takes a bit to get into the game and make the best of all the options the game presents to you and find the best way to get ahead in the race that is Euphoria. While the board is a little too busy for my taste and I would have preferred more readable dice, there are a lot of cool ideas in it so that Euphoria doesn't feel like the umpteenth worker placement game.

I think that it's a great idea that you should contribute to building locations in order to prevent their negative effects. Would have been great if you would know from the beginning what it's effect is, but that is just a minor quibble I have. Normally you build stuff to get better things, here it is like reverse engine building. Nice.

I do also like the pulling back of workers and especially the "bumping" places where you get workers back if someone else or you use that place again. Brilliant idea that I would love to see implemented in other games too. Makes you really think if you do really need that space if it would help someone else getting back their worker. On the other hand it allows for some clever play if someone has an already intelligent workforce and that one worker they would have to take back might make them lose a worker. I learned this small tactic the hard way. Brilliant stuff.

What I'm not so sure about is how much luck there is in this game. Take for instance the artifact draw in which the winner of my one game constantly managed to draw two matching cards. This gave him a small boost against the other players. But in the end it wasn't too bad since I only was one point behind him. I hope I'll get another chance to see for myself how big of an role luck plays in this game.

RATING: 4
At the end of the game it was clear another player would win in two turns and it would take me three. We decided to play it out, quickly. I rolled doubles so I got to take two actions at once.

I won a two-hour euro with a 3.1 rating on the BGG weight scale and an average rating of 7.4 [i]because I rolled doubles. 

Really.[/i]

I suspect the high average rating is an artifact of the aggressive stretch goals in the kickstarter campaign. People either forget or are reluctant to admit that the game they paid so much for, and hyped the hell out of it so they would get better bits, turned out a mess. The bits, however, do look pretty good.

RATING: 5
Players are racing to be the first society to place 10 stars onto the game board.

Worker placement game, where the hook here is the workers are dice you roll.  High numbers denote increased intelligence, and if your worker pool total is too high, they become aware they are in a dystopia and leave.  You need to shock/hydrate a new worker into life to replace them.

Other than that, it's standard place, accumulate, convert and generally mince stuff to that stars are popped out the other end.  Lots of random penalities if you get caught in the wrong place at the wrong time. 

I didn't care for this - an excellent example of a modern average Kickstarter worker placement game, where the bloom is truly off the rose.  It is interesting to compare this with the recent Russian Railroads, also a worker placement game, but from a professional publisher - the difference in game play quality is astonishing.  

Euphoria does have excellent production though - the pieces are amazing.

RATING: 8.11
Beautiful, no, wonderful game! Great worker placement with beautiful dystopian theme and clever use of dice as workers. Number on a die represent the knowledge of a worker and if worker is too smart he might deflect and that is not good. You need you workers dumb and happy. Board game is vivid, colourful and eventful, with 4 different factions living in a world whose allegiance the players will fight for.

The game is, in its essence, a race for influence and control and it ends when first player places his 10th influence marker (star). Game flows brilliantly in continuous turns, but only until last few turns when the winner is obvious which makes those turns quite redundant. So far, none of the games had exciting finish. I guess that might be changed with more plays, especially because the game is very very enjoyable.

[b]Update:[/b] As I presumed, with more plays every game became more and more exciting and unpredictable until the very end.
Upgraded from 8.5 to 9.

RATING: 7
Reminds me a lot of Manhattan Project, but I feel the implementation of theme is a little weaker here.  Of course, you don't play this kind of game for theme.

Like in Manhattan Project, I immensely enjoy the strategic choice of when and how many workers you take back into the pool.  Unfortunately, on a personal level, this seems to never work out in my favor since I am, and I quote, "quite ballsy" and like to gamble.  That's not a hit against the game, of course.

I think this is a pretty great game with tons of paths towards victory and none of them feel all points-salady.  Like any other Euro, you gotta figure out what you want to do and just stick to executing it.  I am far too often too tempted to try everything and master nothing, which just means I'm always trailing.

However, a big strength of the game is that no matter what we pursued, be it single-minded or multi-faceted, the scores remained pretty close.  To me, that just screams balance.

RATING: 6
Euphoria is a dice placement euro where players are trying to rid themselves of 10 stars... or attain 10 VP... however you want to look at it. There are a number of interesting aspects to Euphoria:

-Each player starts with 2 unique player powers (though one is active and the other is face-down and inactive). Each of these player powers are associated with 4 of the game's factions. These factions can be advanced to help unlock greater benefits for players who own player powers of the associated faction.

-There are areas that players can construct on their own or collectively. When constructed, a new worker placement slot opens and players who didn't contribute to the construction are affected by it's specific negative ability. Players can, however,  take actions to alleviate these conditions.

-There is a system of pulling your workers or placing a worker. When pulling, you have to be careful to not try an exceed a certain number of pips between your dice and a track of fictitious pips, or you will be penalized. How can you be careful with dice rolling? Well, you can pull less than your total number of dice and you can manage a track that affects your pip value. There are a number of worker places that can hold infinite dice or doesn't block people from placing their own dice. When a player is kicked, it's actually somewhat beneficial because they can go longer without having to pull dice, unless this causes you to exceed the pip value. In fact, you can kick your own workers to extend the time needed before pulling... though these spots tend to require resources, so you won't be able to do them infinitely.

There is more that could be said, but just know that you're going into a game with a plethora of options, but is also fairly random. This randomness could be quite a bother to people. Here are some random aspects...

-Dealt player powers are not created equally.
-Rolling doubles can essentially allow you to do a double turn, which ends up being very beneficial
-Rolling too high can cost your some of your dice workers
-If more than one person starts with the same faction of player powers, they can collectively build that faction up quicker, to gain additional benefits quicker.
-Certain construction's negative affects can randomly screw you more than it screws other people, depending on the board situation.
-There is a card collection mechanism. It's beneficial to get sets and certain aspects of the game want specific cards, which you randomly may not get for a while.

Despite the randomness, I find it to be a fairly enjoyable experience. There are even also supposed to be some official variants that, I believe, can reduce some of the random aspects mentioned above. That said, I haven't read them. Regardless, the game is quicker to play than most worker placement games (especially considering it can go up to 6), includes a fair amount of variety (at least with regards to the player powers and constructions), has less blocking and seemingly more options than most games in this genre, has nice components/graphic design, and seems to be fairly easy to teach. Unfortunately, dice euros are becoming a crowded field with many great entries, but this still might be worthwhile entry depending on your preferences.

RATING: 6
I had a big helping of Beginner’s Luck here as I never lost a worker and people did a lot of things that benefitted me at just the right time. My starting card gave me a benefit when others rolled doubles, and doubles they rolled. Consequently, I can’t help but wonder about how dependent the game is on luck and how balanced the cards are. You get a special power card at game start, and the one I got worked out very well, especially as compared to the other ones I saw during the game. Furthermore, seems like there’d be a large advantage to one player starting the game with two cards of the same faction (though that’d be easy to house rule if that’s the case). 

At first the game seems quite opaque due to numerous places where you can play, but you really only need to focus on one area that complements whatever guild you’re working. You want to find an efficient way to place stars on the board as it’s a race in that regard, and I’m just not sure after one play if there’s depth here or just a lot of different things that, once experienced, will feel same-y such that it will come down to card pulls and who rolls better. From the comments I read of others, it seems that's the case.

RATING: 5
Fantastic presentation and at first glance an interesting theme. I wanted to like this game.

But... the game is very basic. And there's precious little connection with the theme. Workers gain knowledge but it evaporates every dice roll. Workers are lost due to knowledge gain, but where do they go? Why is 'building a better dystopia' achieved by controlling markets for antiques? 

Euphoria's niche role is as a quick playing worker placement race to place ten authority markers. 

Satisfactory, I suppose, but I don't think Euphoria has the legs or charm to warrant repeat play. An inferior worker placement option, with little to recommend it other than its presentation.

Revised character cards included.

RATING: N/A
I love, love, love:
- the race style of gameplay
- the dark? humor
- the component quality and artwork
- the gorgeous main board and that the resources don't take away from the artwork when added to the board

I dislike:
- the rather blah Artifacts 
- that losing out on the markets can really punish players (I don't agree with this idea of beating down on someone who's already behind--the player has missed out on a star and is falling behind, so why punish someone that's falling behind even more?)
- that every ethical dilemma results in the same two choices (Why even bother with making so many cards that all give the same results? Either design this part of the game differently or don't bother at all.)
- that the theme isn't THAT strong, despite there being a story to try to make the mechanisms/board logical)

Final verdict: Need more plays...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Worker placement is apparently a genre that is still evolving. You think you've seen it all, and then the next game comes along and proves you wrong. In this case, adding more workers to your pool increases the risk that you have to return one again when you retrieve them. It's a bit of a push-your-luck element in an unexpected place. The rest of the game is the old cycle of collecting resources and then turning them into victory points, so not something to induce euphoria although it works just fine.

It's a good idea to give players asymmetrical starting positions and different incentives in a game like this but the recruits in Euphoria don't seem to be very balanced and can easily tilt the game right from the start, just like the rule for rolling doubles strongly favours those with lucky rolls during the game.

RATING: 7
Mixed feelings after one play.

Conclusion
Lots of good things in a game with lots of strategy that does not take more then 60 min to play. That is superawesome, but there are too many strategy elements and if you are unlucky with one of them you will not win. There is a push your luck element so you can choose to take smaller risk, but if you are unlucky it is really bad and you get nothing in return for your bad luck. There is no way to really mitigate the randomness. Therefore, I would like to play it again, but I would much rather play Scythe or Viticulture from the same designer. 

Good
+ Fun theme, although the game is not heavy on theme. 
+ Nice components, very nice dice (although they functions just as regular d6 dice)
+ Easy to play once you learn it. 
+ Quick turns.
+ Strategic depth in rather short time
+ Nice combos that improve your actions. When you do something you often get something more or something else happens that is good for you if you are friends with a faction. 
+ Action placement where you can push workers home and if you do not have any workers home you need to spend a turn taking home workers. If you can foresee what other players want to do you get your workers back without doing an action. That is a great mechanism. 
+ Get one resource type to do another action to trade that to another resource type and with that resource you can build something is ok, combined with other worker placement spots it is nice.
+ When something is build everyone who built part of it get a star (VP) and those who didn´t get something bad. (I would have prefered to get something good if you build it but still good)
+ When you take some actions you get 1 resource and something good happens if die sum is 1-4. If die sum is 5-8 you get 1 resource and something else good happens, if die sum is 9 or more you get 2 resouces and something bad happens. This works really well and make you pick a specific die on a specific action so that you get what you want. 

Bad
- Randomness can have too big effect on outcome. (but didn't in the game I played, I won so it was obviously pure luck)
- Roll two of the same and you get a free turn. 
- Roll too high and you will loose a worker. That could hurt alot, if it happens when you have 2 dice you will not win the game. 
- Starting cards are very unbalanced and even though you get 4 and pick 2 you may not get a great start card.
- 3 Factions in the game are exaclty the same, that makes it easier to make decitions but makes the theme feel a little thin. 
- Quite difficult rules to explain. 
- Hidden buildings can be very bad for you not to build or really does not matter at all
- Text on hidden buildings is small and it is hard to remember that something bad happens to you when you roll a 3 because you have not got a star on a building. 
- Very dependent on other players actions and luck when it comes to what factions they like. If you are the only one who like a faction you will not win.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
Stunningly beautiful game bits.  Gameplay is chaotic, with many bad surprises.  For example, in our 6p game, only 4 could be in on each great work, and the penalty could be mild, or quite nasty, like losing workers every time you get a certain die roll.  Yikes!  You don't even know what building you're working on until after it's completed and revealed.  Oh, and rolling doubles (or triples) means you taken extra turns with no cost?  It's really the antithesis of strategy.  Just roll well.

There's subtlety and nuance to placing workers due to how they can stay out there or be bumped back to your active supply, and a bit of odd alliance when others want to bump your faction or other objective.  All in all, however, the chaos overwhelms the strategy, making for more frustration than a sense of puzzling things out.

RATING: 7
Euphoria : Build a Better Distopia is a worker placement with dice game for 2 to 6 player.
One your turn, you either place a worker or remove one or more worker.  You do the action when you place worker.  You can bump previously place worker belonging to any player including yourself.  Each time you retrieve worker, you roll to see their knowledge and add the score to your society knowledge.  If it's too high, one of them realize that he's in a distopia and leave.
You also have 2 recruit that belong to one of the four allegiance.  These recruit give unique bonuses throughout the game.  One of your recruit is active at the start of the game while the other is not.  There is a common track for each of the four allegiance.  When the marker for each allegiance reach a certain point, it gives bonuses to the players who have an active recruit of this allegiance.  Its also when the allegiance track reach a certain point that player can activate their other recruit.
The first player to place his 10 authority token win the game.

The game can be overwhelming at first because of all the stuff on the board and there is so many things that can be done but after a few turns it become very fluid.  The first game I played was a 3 player game and it took us 60 minutes to play the game.  It was way quicker than I was expecting!  The game is fast, there is no downtime which is great!  It's probably my best 6 player game that plays in less 90 minute.

RATING: 7
After one play, it's clear the theme is tenuous because the mechanics are predominantly worker placement. What makes it  interesting is the penalty system of the market cards—if a player does not participate in unlocking a market, that market will cause him to suffer a penalty for the rest of the game—he won't be able to take a particular action or earn a given bonus.

That means the players have to pay constant attention and not necessarily try to race to win, because they may find themselves crippled right at the end of the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A strange and loose worker placement game with very little blocking. I had some fun with it, but it's more complex and much more random than it needed to be.

The use of dice as workers mostly serve to give players extra turns by rolling doubles and to make them lose workers when rolling too high (which in practice means losing several turns). There are also random buildings that penalize players who don't participate in their construction, but the penalties vary from very mild to really harsh, and they are only revealed when the building is completed. Interaction is usually indirect and involuntary: for example, you get special power cards from various factions, and having the same faction as an opponent can be very beneficial. Artifact cards are completely random draws and you simply are lucky or not to get pairs and specific artifacts you need.

The dystopia theme is interesting, but the game feels very abstract. You basically get commodities and convert them into resources that then you exchange for a victory point for some unexplained reason. Also, the art style does nothing for me.

There might be a good game in there, but the lack of development and poor design decisions prevent it from rising above mediocrity, pretty much like the designer's previous effort, "Viticulture".

RATING: 6.8
Pros: The theme is really fun in this game and the bumping mechanism is well done.  I absolutely love the use of dice as workers and the "knowledge" limitations placed on the workforce.

Cons: Some of the actions seem useless for less than 4 or 5 players.  The game plays especially poorly with 2 players since there are so many options that players can almost entirely avoid confrontation.  While the theme fits well with the workers games quickly fall into a generic resource conversion game.  Penalties from not participating in building markets can be easy to track during the game.

Overall, good game and lots of funny moments if you are willing to run with the theme.

A good medium/light weight worker/dice-placement game, but it's fairly generic.
The thing that differentiate it from most worker placement games is that you can't block spaces from other players (instead, the "blocking" worker is returned to the owner). That sort of removes most of the tension and makes the game a lot more "multiplayer solitaire"-ish.
Using dice as workers doesn't really affect the game play much. It's more of a gimmick.

RATING: 7.25
Euphoria is a very clever die placement game, with multiple paths towards victory that eventually boils down to area control.

Players try to allocate all their tokens onto the board, by completing structures, trading in markets, recruit influence, or other activities.

To place a token generally requires resources, currencies, and/or a free worker to use. But the number of workers is very limited, so the worker allocation choices are tricky, and planning for actions is important.

Having the resources to claim a spot at the right time is key; miss out too much and you'll be watching your opponent's backside when they win.

The kickstarter bits are among the best I have ever seen

I liked the game a lot on a first play; I am starting the rating for Euphoria at a 7.25.

There are a lot of choices, and it will be interesting to sort out strategies and paths to victory.

RATING: N/A
ユーフォリア
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[b]tl;dr[/b] — An ultimately tedious affair allowed to go on too long only because it was beautiful.  Problems arose early in our 17 plays, but we chalked those up to our lack of experience, choosing to trust the designer's vision and skill rather than assume a lack of development.  Turns out we were wrong.  Unfortunately half-baked. 

[microbadge=28503]

[b]T[/b]he handpicked crowd was cheering, as they were obligated to do, when we met for the last time at the podium.  We smiled like the serpents we were and clasped hands as murderous apes must, and [i]sotto voce[/i], beyond the reach of the mic, you breathed:

"I'll see you shot or hanged.  Maybe both."

My smile broadened as I clapped you on the back and turned to the auto-exaltation of the crowd.  "That would be just the thing, wouldn't it," I hissed through my teeth, "Just the thing for a [i]traitor[/i]."

All that work for all those years, from the moment of dark enlightenment through the weaving of our self-culling networks, our ambitions entwining and diverging as power increased and our foes fell away, all that work to be the one wearing Orwell's boot—[i]forever[/i]—undone in a blink as the terrorist bomb ripped through the reviewing stand.

Our memorial statues were revealed that same day, the day the new regime promised to bring the haters of freedom to a terrible justice, for that is what, above all, the Republic stands for.

[microbadge=28503]

Mechanically, this is just a [i]grind nibs to trade for nobs and score dinguses[/i] worker-placement affair... but I'm not here for any of that.  Just know that all the mechanical stuff works, and works just fine.

What sets this apart, though, is the theme and the way that theme is brilliantly woven into the proceedings.  For example, everyone begins the game with a revealed recruit from one of four factions, as well as a hidden recruit to be revealed later.  Players sharing the same faction will end up working semi-cooperatively—helping to dig the same tunnel, pushing the same allegiance track forward to increase their faction's power.  The unrevealed recruits add the wrinkle of undeclared alliances—if my mole is the same faction as your active recruit then I know we're kinda in cahoots*... something of which you're not necessarily aware.  [i]Delicious[/i].

[size=7]*At least until I go for placing my final authority star and have you cast head-first and handcuffed into the Pit of Justice.[/size]

There are so many other bright things I could point to—the unrestricted trade of commodities, resources and artifacts; colluding to freeze other players out of constuction sites; the way low morale hinders your ability to collect artifacts (disaffected workers will rat you out to superiors); and on and on and on...

The attention to detail and the organic cohesion of theme, mechanics, and presentation are breathtaking.

I could very easily see this becoming one of my favorite games—instead of farming to impress the king how 'bout if I impress myself by lobotimizing some workers?

[i]Get in the machine, you![/i]

UPDATE:  Enjoying this enough that I popped for the "Supreme" version, both to get the up-rezzed bits (gold bricks!  [i]brick[/i] bricks!  tiny stones!) and to get the "faceless" recruits as I'm deathly allergic to [thread=1059815]"girlfriend mage"[/thread].  You see, the game comes with fish-out-of-water backer faces inserted into the art; not being an actual backer myself, I figured the "faceless" recruits would look like these:

[imageid=1901168 original]
But they don't...  And one is even a baby—[i]a baby[/i].

[i]Sigh.[/i]  The moral of the story is that there's no reason to splurge and scramble for an aftermarket "Supreme" edition—"Deluxe" works just fine if you want the super-fancy stuff.

UP-UPDATE:  In actual play I find that the backer-face recruits don't annoy me as much as I thought they would; in fact, the little bit of personality they add to the game is... actually kinda nice, I suppose.  Still, if they ever end up offering the original faceless ones I'd grab 'em, no hesitation.

PS.  If you're wondering what to do with those allegiance bonus markers, try [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/15121843#15121843]this[/url].

UP-UP-UPDATE:  The luck factor can only be mitigated through nepotism—as you fall behind and/or are cruelly afflicted by The System you must get busy wheeling and dealing through unrestricted trade.  Don't be shy about greasing palms and maintaining those connections... for only when you find yourself in that place where you have no friends are you truly hosed.

[b]After 15 plays:[/b]  8 --> 7  Start positions are precipitously uneven and unknowable until the game begins, at which point you are either unjustly rewarded or iniquitously punished until it's over.  I still like what it does, it's just that sometimes being the dystopic object lesson—[i]again[/i]—is hard to take.

FINAL ASSESSMENT:  7 --> 6  Wore this out in 17 plays.  Far too much of how the game will go for you lies in which recruits you get in the initial deal:  Pick the same factions as others at the table (especially if you get a pair of a popular faction) who also give you freebies or otherwise enhance the economics of any single die placement and your game will be [i]awesome[/i].  If not, you will lose—good play notwithstanding—to opponents of equal skill who lucked-out with better recruits.  And there's just no good fix for this.  I'll keep it around, but we're done with it for now.

Includes:  [thing=172882]16 Updated Recruit Cards[/thing].

RATING: 7.3
規則:
1.日用品圓的(4)，資源方的(3)，按人數放未開放領土指示物(幾人有幾格可以占)，隨機洗六個建設地點蓋著放置，聯盟指示物跟礦工放在各自起始位置，通到盡頭放未開放指示物
2.起始一人四張招募卡，選兩張，一張開著一張蓋著，剩下兩張棄掉，剩餘的成牌庫，一張倫理困境卡，民心軌1，知識軌3，兩個工人，骰完數字最大為起始玩家，一人一個倍增標記
3.
A:遊戲行動放工人，如果工人骰點數一樣，可以一回合連放(如果是因為行動回手的不行)，放完付費用(灰方框)，得好處(圓)
B:收工人，收多少隨意，有付食物或雲加民心，沒付扣民心，民心影響文物卡上限
C:卡支付倫理困境卡上資源翻面或得好處
4.淺灰虛線方框可踢人使用，實線大框不限人數，實線小框先搶先贏不能踢
5.民心不夠扣還是可以拿回工人，但是如果是招募卡交換就需要有足夠的民心扣
6.每次重骰要結算知識，手上的工人+知識大等於16要棄最大值的骰
7.多數行動即使沒辦法全拿還是可以只拿部分來踩，但是有辦法拿就要拿
8.日用品區是看大格內全部知識總合來決定拿到什麼
**資源 日用品 文物卡可交易
9.聯盟忠誠軌有獎勵，且向下累積，只要有該聯盟招募卡即可享有，第三級可開啟隱藏招募卡，另外就是挖礦也可開啟隱藏招募卡，四級可放星到招募卡
10.建造市場 2-3p:2 4p:4 5-6p:5 占滿才可建造完成，有參予的可放星，沒參與要懲罰，
11.文物市場可放星到領土或市場，市場只能放星到領土
12.所有可以支付三個文物卡的行動，可以改成支付兩張一樣的文物卡
13.先放10星獲勝，平手檢定 民心>知識>有星市場數目>有星領土數目>擲骰+知識軌 最低獲勝
14變體 招募卡倫抽 市場設置，民心知識起始位置

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This game is a race to place all 10 of your stars by at various locations on the board, whoever does so first wins. It is a dice worker placement game, but there is little/no blocking in terms of doing an action (you just bump someone off the spot and they get their die back), but in certain spots the amount of pips there slightly affects how many of a resource you collect.

There are 6 "markets" (buildings) that can be built, which is where you will probably try to put 6 of your stars. If you help contribute to building it (one of the listed materials), you get a star there when the building is done. If you did not help, you do not place a star and will receive a random penalty for the rest of the game (whatever is listed on the tile) UNTIL you place a star on that building via a different action. The penalties are generally not crippling (ie: if you ever roll a "1", lose a resource or commodity, etc), but you are best served being sure you are a part of the other buildings than trying to catch up first on buildings you missed out on.

The amount of resources needed to complete a market is based on player count, so there will always be at least one player who does not get a chance to help build the building. However, in a 6 player game, there will be at least 2 people who do not get a chance to help build each market, so it takes longer to place stars, which causes the game to drag. (which is why generally not recommended to play with 6.)

RATING: 6
Euphoria adds a lot of complexity to the dice-rolling worker placement genre, but in my opinion it falls short in the all-important fun factor. No avoiding the obvious comparison to Alien Frontiers, which is a much tighter, more enjoyable game.  Euphoria just seems to make everything difficult to do for the sake of being difficult - commodities must be converted to resources which must be converted to markets which finally get you points, or you have to go through several laborious sequences of steps in order to get cards to get...and so on and so on.  Alien Frontiers also makes the dice rolls more interesting since the roll values have meaning, whereas in Euphoria it's more just about avoiding negative consequences of having high rolls (other than a few specific instances where the roll value matters for placement purposes).  Euphoria isn't a bad game and I would play it again, but I think Alien Frontiers bests it in pretty much every way.

RATING: 6
16 October, 2016:  Here's a diarrhoea of thoughts on my very first experience at playing Euphoria today in a 6-player game, which was no where near as euphoric as I had hoped.

I took some issues to this game for several reasons.

First of all, the game board - blimey! - it's like someone vomited everything they could think of and thought "What a brilliant idea!"  Good fricking grief!!!  Don’t get me wrong; It’s nicely designed and relatively pretty to look at, but it’s chaotic and is a clusterf#ck (pardon my french) to look at.  If I had familiarity with the board, it wouldn’t be a problem.  However, for a first timer at this game, I was NOT IMPRESSED.

There were many, many mechanics to remember in this game.  Examples include: If your dice roll plus the location of your knowledge track equals to 16 or higher, you lose a worker.  When you retrieve your workers, you can pay a commodity to increase your hand size or decrease your hand size (unless your hand size is already at 1, which is the lowest).  Or any time you are bumped from a location from another player you must re-roll your die, or dice if you still have unused dice in your pool.  Or if a factory was built but you did not help build it, you have to remember to take your penalty under specific conditions until you’ve fulfilled certain requirements that would help eliminate the penalty.  There were a lot of things going on, and each time we were reminded so frequently (at nearly every 5-6 minutes interval) it feels like we were being nagged.  It makes for an unpleasant experience over the course of the game and, for me, it made me feel snappish at being constantly policed.  It was as though I can’t be trusted to think (because I’m already compromised by having to remember so many nuances in the game).

I enjoy learning a new game (on most days).  And my enjoyment increases exponentially when the flow of the game enhances the learning experience, which means that the game is captivating and I feel involved in it even when it’s someone else’s turn.  In a game like Euphoria where there are many options with an even higher likelihood of analysis-paralysis (in gamers old and new), the SMARTER strategy to introducing this game and many of its’ ilk would be to start with a lower number of players so that new players won’t have to contend with additional players’ chaos on the board and new players won’t have to sit on their thumbs for too long before they forget what they wanted to do when it’s their turn again.  Less players in an introduction game also means less verbal clutter across the table, and less overall time it takes to play the game in its entirety.  I've no doubt that with more experience and familiarity the gameplay can occur in relatively quick succession, even in a 6-player game.

In retrospect, I can’t even say that there were many moments of laughter directly as a result of the game and our direct gameplay interactions with each other.  Any time we had laughed during the game was a result of someone’s verbal brouhaha (i.e. the head tokens used along the knowledge track was verbalised as “going down on the head” until we finally found a more p.c.-friendly phrase).

My overall impression of Euphoria is that the designers wanted to incorporate many game mechanics that they enjoyed but they forgot to STOP, take a step back and think, “This might’ve been overkill” or “We need to scale this back a bit.”  This is not a good entry game at all;  This is a game you want to bring to the table if you don’t mind one to five people holding a grudge against you for the next 12-months, at minimum.  Despite my reservations about Euphoria, I would CAUTIOUSLY give this game another chance.  Depending on my mood, I might need to be bribed.

RATING: 7
Euphoria is a solid worker placement game with some very interesting mechanisms, including the metagame of figuring out which player has an interest in advancing factions that benefit oneself, the ability to use occupied spots if willing to return a worker back to the player who occupies said spots, and the luck-pushing mechanism that arises when attempting to acquire and keep more workers.  Combine these unique twists with an offbeat theme and the result is an interesting spin on traditional worker placement.

RATING: 4
2014-02-21

Initial Rating: 4.0 (February 2014)

After the explanation, I had high hopes for the game, sadly my expectations were utterly crushed. Euphoria is a pretty boardgame, with some thematic touches that are very nice. Loved the text on the Ethical Dilemma cards, the art is great. It has cool dice (although I thought they were custom dice, instead they are regulars D6s with pretty gears on them) and great graphic design. The games flows quickly with 4 players. The rules are fairly easy to grasp and the great board design helps a lot to understand the game. I like the resource components and even liked the spreadsheet to multiply and track your resources.

What kills it for me, is the incredible amount of uncontrollable luck (for a supposedly strategy game) you get in the game. Beginning with the pair rule, why should I gain an action if I rolled a pair (trio, poker, etc.) This is very unfair, I know that if I roll more dice I have a better probability to achieve this, but I still rank it as very unfair. Also hate the luck with the artifacts, if I draw a couple of them I can do many powerful actions when other players need 3 to do the same. The recruits powers are very unbalanced, and it is luck if you get a good one or not. Worst of all are the buildings, as they are face down you don’t know which ones are powerful, the negative effects of some buildings are very damaging. Even if the buildings were face up, some player will get hosed more by luck than by skill (player order, if I roll pairs, etc.) and be left out of that building. The knowledge (amount of rolled dice) doesn’t seem very relevant, they are only used on the mines and to see if I lose a worker. Almost never this is a real consideration and you can always use them (without any regard to the level of knowledge) as you wish (no tension, pure luck). The game punishes you if you have more than 3 workers as you will probably lose them, so I almost never will take back more than 3 workers. My point is, that it is (almost) completely irrelevant what numbers do you roll on your dice (except when you roll pairs :(). You have available a lot of dice spaces, but you always use the same few ones, ,meaning, it has no interesting decisions. 

The ethical dilemma cards are cool, but there is no really a motivation to choose the recruit over the VP, I know some times it will be valuable (if you managed to draw a very powerful recruit) but most times it makes more sense to get the VP, also it is pure luck if you get a good recruit (or an advanced one) or not.

Bottom line, Euphoria is a gorgeous luck fest, that it is way too long for what it offers. There is no tension whatsoever and the luckiest player will win as most decisions are obvious. Perhaps the worst dice/strategy game I have ever played. I hope the person who explained the game to me made a lot of mistakes, otherwise I won’t ever play this luck fest again. I’m rating it highly due to its great components.

RATING: 6
This is a very preliminary rating, after only one 2 player game. Euphoria is a pretty good worker placement game with a nice twist on the mechanic. There's no "blocking" per se, but you taking an occupied space can sometimes the opponent who was already there. The strategy in the game is really wide open; there are many possible paths to victory, but each path bears a strong resemblance to all of the others. I suspect that this is another game that shines with more players, but I still enjoyed it with 2, and I'm looking forward to playing it again.

Update: I have since played this with 6 players and I do think it plays much better at higher player counts. The lack of blocking eliminates any kind of real tension, at least until the end of the game when everyone is closing in on victory. It absolutely feels like a race. I can't say my rating has gone up, but I do want to play it some more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Nice game with some interesting ideas.  I would rate it much higher if it weren't for one fatal flaw.  And I do mean fatal.

At the beginning everyone secretly chooses two of four recruits dealt to them. Each recruit has an allegiance to one of four areas.  Players who, by random chance, choose recruits from the same areas will inevitably help each other by working towards the same goals, while players who, by random chance, choose recruits from areas different than those chosen by others will find themselves working alone and have no hope of victory.  In other words, you can literally lose this game by your choice of recruits before the start, with no relevant information to guide your choices.  This kills the game from the start, and for that reason alone I won't be playing it again.

RATING: 4
I imagine the idea for Euphoria started over a game of Agricola, where one player said, "What if these workers were DICE? Wouldn't that be cool??" Well, no it isn't. Not the way it was implemented. The idea is that you roll your workers every time you collect them and if you roll over a certain amount, then you lose one. That's all the values accomplish. That's it. The publisher seems to have built an entire theme around that idea, calling the pips "knowledge" and that too much knowledge is dangerous and makes your workers disappear. However in reality, knowledge is something that is gained and added to over time, not randomly determined every time you wake up. Almost everything about this game feels cobbled-together or tacked-on to the point of annoyance.

This is a game of small, incremental advancements in order to be the first player to get 10 points. The game plods along as you place a single die and accomplish something small every turn. The turns are quick, at least. The only real tension in the game is whether you will lose a worker when you roll the dice. Otherwise, the usual tension of worker placement games (taking an action before somebody else can) is gone since you can simply bump anybody you want out of most spaces. There are action spaces that are built throughout the game, giving more opportunities for points. However, if you didn't contribute to the building of a space, something bad, sometimes REAL bad, happens to you. Usually it takes the form of restricting you in some way ("loose a resource when you roll X", or "you no longer get allegiance bonuses of the 1st or 2nd tier"). These restrictions are more depressing than interesting. You won't know how bad a tile is until it is turned over, so pray you contribute to the right tiles, because there is no way to know.

For a game that has the trappings of strategy, there is far too much random bad stuff that can happen TO you and no tension in the action selection mechanisms. I find myself at the mercy of the game, much as my dice do when I order them to go mine some bliss. They should just use their intelligence and tell me to screw off.

The game has been published after a very worked crowdfounding campaign that has made possible its amazing components, but lately it is overproduced, and after all the board and the dices are not clear.

RATING: 5
Listened to the hype and boy was I fooled. I understand why new gamers would like it and I would have loved it when I first got back into gaming in 2003.  However there are just so many better games than this undercooked game with great components ( I think this is why many people love this game).  Several Fatal flaws; random buildings that one helps build but one doesn't know the building or the benefits until it is built.  Some of the buildings have brutal penalties if you don't help build them can't buy certain goods, can't visit certain areas, lose goods if you roll a 3 (what random nonsense) while other buildings penalties seem very tame.  You could pick six builds randomly that help one or two factions and not the others and the lack of balance means if you picked the wrong faction from the beginning of the game you can be hosed. 

 just a few other problems include major card balance issues (some cards are just way too powerful endgame cards that allow a player to place two stars with one action.). Getting extra workers requires water or electricity which benefits two factions that get bonuses when selecting water or electricity.  
Laughable that people call this a deep game. No way.

This needed more play testing by experienced gamers with hundreds of games under their belt.  I have played four times and will try some more but boy I would be excited to trade this bore.  

This is the last straw no more kickstarter games until I play them first.

RATING: 5
The professionality of Jamey Stegmaier in setting up this Kickstarter convinced me to back my first and so far only project. In terms of production value and on-time-delivery this is an A+. When it comes to gameplay value, it's more of a mixed bag. Euphoria has seen development and still is taken care of with minor adjustments to the rules. It's not a shipwreck like most other Kickstarter games I have played before. But it clearly doesn't have a professional level of development. It's also a noticeably American take on Eurogames in its randomness.
The main offender is the importance of the allegiance tracks for winning. Players sharing an allegiance and jointly promoting the track will have a major advantage to others left alone with an allegiance. The decision to focus on the action areas of your allegiances is too obvious to be interesting. The problem is that the choice of allegiance for the biggest part is done secretly at game start not leaving much room to compensate being stuck alone.
The game can be unbalanced even more from the start by noticeably differing powers of the recruits. Within the game, unlucky dice rolls (losing workers, not getting doubles) can further skew the game without much influence for the players. This all would be fine within the announced 60 minutes playtime but our play lasted more than 2 hours with four players.
Still, I'll play again. There are a few elements interesting enough such as the shared building of markets or a real decision whether to invest in additional workers that might be lost again. The material of the resources also entices to be played with.

RATING: 4
[b]First Impressions:[/b] I played a two player game and enjoyed it. I feel more depth and enjoyment would come with more players though, which is really saying something, as I enjoyed the first game.

[b]7/24/2014 Update[/b] 
After playing with more people, I've come up with some observations.

First, the game starts off feeling very unguided and slow. As the game grows, the pace starts to pick up. But by the time the game is over, it feels like the game went on about 30 minutes longer than it should have.

The game is also a little too fiddly. It's difficult to always keep track of all the things that can shift and change, even though 3 of the 4 factions all work in a similar way, it all adds up to a lot of things you need to keep track of, making it feel very fiddly.

But yet - it's still a very fun game.

RATING: 7
What a marriage of worker placement and dice rolling. Unlike Alien Frontiers the workers have a different but still interesting mechanic and I think the game requires a certain type of personality to enjoy the dystopian setting in its many hidden humorous winks and nudges.
I really like the design, even though the board and all its components seem very overloaded and there is just too much going on which result in information overload. However, after a few plays you can actually start out with a strategy.
As with many worker placement games you need to know this game in order to enjoy it and understand its deeper strategic elements (even though dice rolling is involved) - there are some minor flaws which can lead to a premature ending if you don't recognize what another player is going for and the markets can screw you over if you go last and didn't notice when the rush for building them has started. Aside from that - a solid game with a whacky theme.

RATING: 5
Worker placement and resource conversion game. Rather than engine building there's a focus on spamming a small set of actions as dictated by variable player powers and periodically securing contributions to resource pools. Player interaction is otherwise somewhat incidental. For a game where you race to a comparatively small number of victory points there's a notable degree of seemingly unmotivated randomness; if I were to play again I would lobby to remove some of that. The theme didn't make itself felt beyond the fact that there are four futuristic factions and fruit. I don't know, it just feels like if Reiner Knizia had done this he'd have distilled everything that's genuinely interesting about it into one or two simple but meaningful processes - and then everyone would dismiss it as a filler.

RATING: N/A
In Euphoria: Build a Better Dystopia, you lead a team of workers (dice) and recruits (cards) to claim ownership of the dystopian world. You will generate commodities, dig tunnels to infiltrate opposing areas, construct markets, collect artifacts, strengthen allegiances, and fulfill secret agendas.

Euphoria is a worker-placement game in which dice are your workers. The number on each die represents a worker's knowledge—that is, his level of awareness that he's in a dystopia. Worker knowledge enables various bonuses and impacts player interaction. If the collective knowledge of all of your available workers gets too high, one of them might desert you. You also have two elite recruit cards at your disposal; one has pledged allegiance to you, but the other needs some convincing. You can reveal and use the reticent recruit by reaching certain milestones in the game... or by letting other players unwittingly reach those milestones for you.

RATING: 4.5
somewhat interesting dystopian thematic touches and trending mechanism ideas (dice instead of workers; taking back your workers is a separate action; your workers can be kicked from the spaces they were placed to, so you get them back and can re-use them etc.). Has some fine ideas but isn't a clear nor an elegant design - and it seems just too much depends on luck. Luck of the dice you roll (you can lose workers because of a bad roll; when rolling double you can have two actions in one turn etc.), luck of the item cards you draw (you need certain cards for certain aims or two similar items but there are six different items and a low hand limit), luck of the aim cards you draw (they seem rather imbalanced) and even some luck in the marketplace tiles built. As it's a game where (just like in simpler games like Settlers of Catan) you play until someone reaches 10 points and it's more luck-dependent than Catan, this thing is just way too complex for this. Oh, and also the four differet areas on the board are rather samey, uninspired and interchangeable (very unlike the four tracks of Russian Railroads for example). I think right now this is my least favorite 2013 game.

RATING: 7
First Impression:

Another enjoyable take on worker placement by Stonemaier Games!  Pretty simple in that you either place a worker and take the action or you return all your workers.  There's just a couple of interesting catches.  On returning workers, you roll your workers and if they total 16 or more, you lose a worker since they became too smart.  On placement, you can often kick people out to take a unique action allowing them or delaying them on rolling their dice, if you roll doubles, you can can place both your workers in the same turn, and in some cases the sum of the dice determine how much you acquire.  Recruits give you special actions and usually extra bonuses as well.  Fun game!  

Be aware that if you don't help build the markets, you may be hurting.  If you don't contribute towards building a market, each of the six buildings have a penalty that is in effect for the rest of the game until you get a star on the market in a different way.

RATING: 6
[microbadge=5]

Worker placement game that's a little too friendly with its worker placement.  There's no real denial of actions since you can just bump people.  There are also some prizes you can invest in which penalize people who don't invest... but then the penalized people can just pay to ignore the penalty.  Much too friendly for this mode of game.  The actual engine of the game is mostly exchanging cubes for different cubes--little compelling theme or narrative.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A wide-open worker placement game that I think suffers from a lack of focus. There are some quasi-cooperative aspects (building new worker spaces) that really hammer players who don't cooperate with ongoing penalties, and it's very easy for a player to get caught behind the curve clearing one penalty while his opponents build the next thing and impose a new penalty.

Of the four board factions, the fluffy-drug-cloud folks seem to have the most flexibility in their actions, which makes their spaces consequently more popular, which makes their cards more valuable. Get dealt those early and you've probably got a leg up. I'll admit that this could be largely a function of group-think.

Finally, the resource push-pull of being able to lose dice means that engine building isn't rewarded. This is an interesting variation on most worker placement games, but ultimately I find it underwhelming. Victory is achieved by doing any number of various things, but win or lose, I can't look at the board, or even my stuff, and feel like I've accomplished anything. Dystopia in boardgame form? Maybe.
