RATING: 7
Played at CotC '14.  Wow, this is such a great worker placement game to start people with.  There's no clean-up, bureaucracy, add resources, take your workers home phases, it just goes round and around and players either place a worker or pay a nominal cost to remove any number of them.  Just a great little game, neat mechanics, not that bad to teach once you get a handle on it.

Traded Archipelago for this on here.  EVEN BETTER it was the Kickstarter version, so includes the upgrade allegiance stars and resources.

Bought the GameTrayz for this in the beginning of 2016, was worthwhile since they had a sale.

Traded away at Origins'16 since the group seemed to kinda be over it. Great for introducing newer players to worker placement, but when do I actually get to break out longer games with anyone but the regulars anymore anyways?

RATING: 4
Playing this game, i felt like an operator in a process control room. Overwhelming board. Game is not bad, but very typical Euro. 

Rather abstract and difficult to get a hold on. In essence it's collecting resources and converting them into points. But the number of options is overwhelming and the relationship between these options is not so easy to find out in a first play.

The game needed a lot of starting up time, before the engines started running and stars could be disposed of.

Typically a game you need to play more than once, but i'm not sure if that will ever happen. Game fun is low. This is for people that like details and complexity. Not for me. 

Gold and brick blocks are really neat (only with kickstarter edition, so it seems). But these gadgets cannot make up for the chaotic board.

The power of the cards you draw seem to have a huge impact on your chances to win the game.

Theme? What theme? This is a "pure mechanism" game. Brain activity without fun.

Minus: 2 - 4 - 5 - 6 - 7 - 9 (not for beginners)

RATING: 7
After a handful of initial plays upon arrival from Kickstarter, this game has sat on the shelf. When selling it recently, I tried to work out why. I think the answer lies in the failure to carry the theme through. An odd comment as theme seems to be everywhere - everywhere except maybe in the feel of the game. The (great) idea of workers gaining knowledge is dealt with in a very abstract way and the sense of doing something subversive or dangerous didn't grip me. There is a great deal to love here and I would want to play it again at some point but it never leapt of the shelf into my arms and thus got canned. From Stonemeier games I will always go with Viticulture/Tuscany, their real masterpiece to date.

RATING: 7
Deluxe Kickstarter Edition.

Excellent components. Solid game. It definitely takes a game to wrap your head around the mechanisms. To be sure, after my wife read me the rules, and I looked out at the board for the first time, I felt completely lost. There's a lot to keep track of, and it's just so different. I think I like it for the following reasons, in order: 

1. Components
2. Originality
3. Gameplay

To be fair, I've only played with 2, and I imagine it is much better with more. The whole "bumping" mechanic really necessitates 4 or more players. Overall, a very good game, one that I'll hold onto for a while, despite its rather large footprint.

RATING: 9
OK, so this is the weirdest game ever! I takes me forever to teach and to make sure that the players grok it (I have on player who just doesn't grok it at all and won't play it because of that). My first games take 2-3 hours depending on the players and the player count, but with experienced players I can get a 5-player game done in around an hour!

I really enjoy (but am crap at) this game: I like dice placement games and games that have dice mitigation (or where the numbers rolled don't matter critically) and I really like the interlocking "engine" elements. However, there are definitely some areas of the game where it's possible to see who will win quite early on as there are some synergies that can arise that are unstoppable. I'm looking forward to the expansion and the way that it is attempting to fix some of those aspects of the game.

(Typical case of the KS-effect: kickstarter backers rated this game already with tens way before its release, so without having played it. As a consequence, enthusiastic gamers that don't look any further than the average will back the game too. In this manner all the stretch goals will be reached because of a collectively created hype. It's a simple but effective mechanism for the first backers to get a box maximized full with stretchgoals.)

RATING: 9
What seems initially like an over-busy (seriously, there is a LOT happening on this board) worker placement game, quickly shows itself as an over-busy worker placement game with some compelling hooks.

One of those hooks is using 'dynamic workers' via a rolled d6 (rather than a static meeple or pawn). This creates a light element of luck and forces you to react to your rolls, as they may change the outcome of some of your actions.

This leads to the most interesting aspect of the game - workers can become too intelligent for their own good and may defect. In most worker placement games, building your engine quickly is often the key to victory. Efficiency and economy of action is paramount in gathering more resources than your opponents.

While that is the case in Euphoria, as those workers will produce more resources, more intelligent workers will also see the dystopian hellscape to which they belong and will leave for greener pastures, forcing you to be out of a potential action until a new cog is shocked or drenched awake.

Because this game takes some expectations of the genre and style and subverts them, this is definitely a game for genre veterans. Newcomers to worker placement style games may feel overwhelmed at the sheer amount of stuff to do and keep track of and may dislike the dynamic worker mechanic and the feeling of 'punishment for doing well' that it can cause.

RATING: 7
Not sure how great this game is but despite many of the familiar mechanics there's a uniqueness about it that I enjoy. Dice are rolled and then used in worker-placement actions. You can gain more dice (workers) but they can be taken away if you roll to high. There's also a little bit of a stock-like game of working together to complete goals. When areas are built they provide extra ways to collect VPs but punish those that did not contribute so timing and paying attention to your opponent's resources is important.

The dystopian theme isn't extremely strong but it's present. Knowledge and heart need to be balanced. Too much knowledge can be dangerous in this world.

RATING: 7

You don't want your rolls too high because you workers will run away. But the way you can manipulate values or lessen risk is very easy to grasp and fits thematically well. I just wish i had the super fancy Kickstarter components but I'm very happy still with the standard components mine came with.

RATING: 8

- very good worker placement game with lots of strategic avenues
- thematic to a degree 
- various elements of luck that usually balance each other out

Negatives
- poor design in starting cards, which despite a draft can still screw you over
SOLUTION: Perhaps instead of 4 random cards for the draft, start with 2 early game cards and 2 late game cards and then do a draft, somehow
- poor design in penalties on closed markets as people not involved in those markets (the people who will logically also not be following that building) get punished
- solution: open buildings/markets and they should give bonuses rather than penalties, rewarding people who helped build them rather than punishing others

RATING: 7
I have played a four player game with friends later that week and really enjoyed it. The game played very well and there was some pretty interesting decisions around worker placement and the scramble to be ‘in’ or, rather, ‘not out’, when a building is finished is also quite engaging. The components are very good, but several of my little lightning bolts have bits falling off the end due, I think, to the wood grain. For a simple to learn game there is a fair bit going on and plenty of interesting choices to be made when there are three other players.

We played two player later the same evening but the experience was a little flatter as there didn’t seem to be any real competition for resources and as we weren’t really doing that much (two tunnels didn’t even get started) in each other’s respective spheres of influence so there wasn’t too much ‘bumping’ of opponent’s dice. Buildings were also built late after we achieved parity in influence in each of the four spheres. It was an okay experience, but based on the one play the game does not shine with two, hence only a rating of 7.

That said, I am pretty happy to have this game and see it getting a bit of use in the future; probably when I have four to six willing players. Well worth considering if any of this sounds your cup of cha.

UPDATE:

Have the extra cards from the Stonemaier  games Treasure box.

RATING: 6.5
Euphoria is a hybrid that's difficult to assess. On one hand, it's a traditional worker placement game, where you use workers to acquire and upgrade resources and exchange them for victory points. On the other hand it has been completed with some interesting elements, such as the player cooperation to dig tunnels and open markets to further common factions, and some less interesting elements, such as die rolls to keep workers and card drawings to build sets.

It feels like some mechanisms, like the the worker knowledge levels (increases randomly and gives risk with little return) and the ethical dilemmas (affect only the player's hand, not the overall game) could have been utilized better.

With few players there seems to be too little room for cooperation and too much room for workers, something that gives luck to much influence. With more players, it's possible that trades and intrigues create more tension and interesting decisions. The production value and the fact that it plays 6 players in a relatively short playing time makes it keeper for now.

I rate the official game a 6 but my personal variant an 7 - take it or leave it. To mitigate the luck and make the worker knowledge ethical dilemmas more interesting, I have added the following rules:

1. Instead of rolling retrieved workers, give them +1. In addition to the collective knowledge check of ≥16, there is an individual knowledge check of ≥7. (Rationale: Workers' learn from their work. Players can plan to modify the workers' intellect before it's too late.)
1a. Starting workers have a total value of 7 (player's choice).
1b. New workers get value 1.
1c. A player's last worker can never get knowledge >6
1d. Recruits and markets referring to die rolls are not used.

2. Instead of using the knowledge track, change individual workers, e.g. +2 knowledge results in 1 worker getting +2 or 2 workers getting +1 each. (Rationale: Workers are dumb but some workers are more dumb than others. Players can choose a knowledge distribution that serves their strategies.)

3. When a worker bumps another worker, the smarter worker gets -1 and the dumber worker gets +1. (Rationale: Workers' intellect is influenced by their company. Players can block attractive actions with smart workers and bump dumb workers.)

4. Instead of drawing unknown artifacts, always keep the 3 top cards face up. Workers with knowledge 1-3 must pick the 1st, workers with knowledge 4-5 may pick the 1st or the 2nd and workers with 6 may pick any. (Rationale: The smarter the worker, the better he searches. Players with smart workers gets an extra benefit to compensate for the risk.)

5. When a player uses an artifact to complete an ethical dilemma, place it face up on the dilemma card to remember. When the specific artifact is drawn again, immediately discard it and get morale +5 (if the choice was to fight the dystopia) or knowledge -5 (if the choice was to contribute to the dystopia).  (Rationale: The artifact is now used to pursue the destiny chosen in the ethical dilemma. This also serves to mitigate the higher worker turnaround due to rule 1.)

Update: Sold because die rolls that you cannot mitigate simply have no place in modern board games.

RATING: 6.5
Not sure this one quite lives up to the raves I've heard. There is kind of an arduous arc to the game -- takes a long time to get any VPs (which, in a twist, come from discarding Lucky Charms-inspired stars) -- then there's a ramping period of VP accumulation -- then, like in 4-player Catan, a period of searching and searching to get the final 2 VPs. At least that was my experience. There is some well thought-out supply chaining in the mix of resources -- but maybe I'm a bit tired of the convert-resources-into-other-resources-or-VPs shtick -- and some interesting mechanics having to do with allies (boosting them, and trying to uncover your secret ally) -- but by mid/late game, all allies will be uncoverable, and most allies will have the benefit of being a star dump. With the dice-as-workers trope in effect, Euphoria's elevator pitch might be "super-Eurofied Alien Frontiers." It's a good game, but while I'm a fan of games with an arc, I think the long ramp-up time and somewhat scripted nature of this particular arc takes down the fundensity a bit.

RATING: 8.5
We really enjoyed our first play. It lasted 3 hours (almost one hour of explanation) for 6 players, but if it can really be played under 1 hour, it would be a huge hit for me.  

Up to now, I've played 5 and 6 player games that have been played in under 2 hours. They didn't feel long, but neither short (also, players were noob to the rules).

The game is not terribly deep, but it's deeper than I thought. 

There's a lot of interaction -not confrontation- in many ways.

The luck factor is not as big as I thought (though I guess in some games some players could always be extra-lucky when rolling doubles).

I have the deluxe version and the components are top-nocht, of course. Probably my best looking game.

My concerns: balance: 
+Are Icarite recruits more powerful than others? They seem to score easily because their actions are highly desirable (especially the bar action).
+The bar action seem to be overpowered (especially if you have an Icarite recruit) you can do it once every 6 actions [assuming 2 workers only, 4 cards, 6 moral and your 2 recruits factions with bonus in phase 1 at least: action 1: 2/3 bliss [min]; action 2: 2/3 other commodity [min]; action 3: retrieve; action 4: bar; action 5 score; action 6, retrieve workers.
+Is it balanced with less than 6 players? Markets were not heavily used with 5-6 players, it seems even more difficult to complete with less players... Doesn't seem to be worth.

***************
Updates about issues (I have played with 2 different groups):
The markets have always been built very scarcely. It's surprising, because they are quite cheap (1 point point per resource). This doesn't concern me very much.
In my third game (CLBSK), the icarites markets were used scarcely. In the 2 previous ones, they were abused, especially the bar that gives you 2 cards.
*************
New update: With 2-3 players, all markets get built pretty soon, and the game lenght decreases a lot, to 1 hour. I enjoyed much more these games.

*************

As for the rating, I start with an 8, and 0,5 more for the components. I will increase it if my concerns turn to out be false and the real playing time with 4 players is 1 hour or less. I will reduce if my balance concerns are justified.

1.日用品圓的(4)，資源方的(3)，按人數放未開放領土指示物(幾人有幾格可以占)，隨機洗六個建設地點蓋著放置，聯盟指示物跟礦工放在各自起始位置，通到盡頭放未開放指示物
2.起始一人四張招募卡，選兩張，一張開著一張蓋著，剩下兩張棄掉，剩餘的成牌庫，一張倫理困境卡，民心軌1，知識軌3，兩個工人，骰完數字最大為起始玩家，一人一個倍增標記
3.
A:遊戲行動放工人，如果工人骰點數一樣，可以一回合連放(如果是因為行動回手的不行)，放完付費用(灰方框)，得好處(圓)
B:收工人，收多少隨意，有付食物或雲加民心，沒付扣民心，民心影響文物卡上限
C:卡支付倫理困境卡上資源翻面或得好處
4.淺灰虛線方框可踢人使用，實線大框不限人數，實線小框先搶先贏不能踢
5.民心不夠扣還是可以拿回工人，但是如果是招募卡交換就需要有足夠的民心扣
6.每次重骰要結算知識，手上的工人+知識大等於16要棄最大值的骰
7.多數行動即使沒辦法全拿還是可以只拿部分來踩，但是有辦法拿就要拿
8.日用品區是看大格內全部知識總合來決定拿到什麼
**資源 日用品 文物卡可交易
9.聯盟忠誠軌有獎勵，且向下累積，只要有該聯盟招募卡即可享有，第三級可開啟隱藏招募卡，另外就是挖礦也可開啟隱藏招募卡，四級可放星到招募卡
10.建造市場 2-3p:2 4p:4 5-6p:5 占滿才可建造完成，有參予的可放星，沒參與要懲罰，
11.文物市場可放星到領土或市場，市場只能放星到領土
12.所有可以支付三個文物卡的行動，可以改成支付兩張一樣的文物卡
13.先放10星獲勝，平手檢定 民心>知識>有星市場數目>有星領土數目>擲骰+知識軌 最低獲勝

RATING: 8
Unique approach to the worker placement genre. A lot in Euphoria is done well: the components, the artwork, and the overall cuteness of the concept are all appealing for players accustomed to ultra-dry Euros. The core mechanics of the game are straight-forward, plus the game forces you to balance out accumulating too many of any one thing: workers, artifacts, and resources. There was never a section of the board I didn't utilize or at least didn't understand which, in my opinion, makes the game's design compact and clear. From the numerous science fiction references to the picture of Viticulture (the designers' last game) as an artifact, the game's clever wit is my favorite part of Euphoria. (As one of my film professors would say about "Hot Fuzz" or any well-made, post-modern art: "It knows where it comes from.") Euphoria is a sly, smart twist on the genre and one I can't wait to play again.

RATING: 10
Holy Resource Management Salad, Batman! 2 interwining paradigms of resources/commodities handling, mixed with worker placement dice of changing intelligence, all to race to achieve 10 authority star tokens within a pseudo-utopia environment.  Yes, that's right: no victory point track here. Though this has worker-placement dice, this is much different than Kingsburg and Alien Frontiers. RATING: 7
5 Plays - Great game, but three things stop me from giving this a near perfect score. 

1. You have to play this game without its, "roll a double and take another move" rule. Unfortunately, using the rule has its benefits though. For example, it helps break up the timing (and therefore predictability) of players moves and allows for exciting dice placing 'combos'. It also factors into the bumping of opponents players workers and retracting workers (e.g. returning an opponents worker to an empty die pool makes it impossible for them to roll a double). A little 'something' IS lost without the rule (as it clearly had purpose in the games' design), but playing with the luck it introduces is just too painful. 2. I would really have liked to have seen EITHER a lot more asymmetry in terms of the different factions worker placement areas or more variety in worker placement options in general. I say this because the game strategies are identical (or perfectly mirrored?) for each faction, with the exception of the cloud faction 3. When you don't contribute to a building you become subject to a penalty - In my playthroughs, these penalties can often be easily  overlooked. The penalties are also particularly brutal, especially if you accumulate a few. So while they're easily overlooked, they also don't often come into play (or stay in play for long). So in practice, they act as more of a threat that prevents 'ignoring the buildings' as a viable option and keeping everyone on a similar path. This in turn makes each playthrough feel even more similar. I feel the mechanic here could be tweaked a bit to make penalties more intuitive for players, as well make buildings a more optional task, allowing players to build different strategies. Again, playing without the 'roll-a-double and get a free move' also helps prevent players from being 'locked out' of a building as a result of a 'dice-placing' combo, making the penalties even more superflous.


What makes me still hold some interest in this game, is its unique take on dice/ worker placement: 1. Retracting workers from the board needs to be balanced with your position on the knowledge track. 2. Placing a dice on an action opens up different cumulative opportunities for yourself and others, encouraging players to 'stack' workers into the same action space for different results. 3. This was one of the earlier designs to include the concept of worker 'bumping', where you can take an action another player has already done, but it allows their worker to 'return' to their worker pool. This in turn builds into point 1 (returning workers and knowledge track position), as well as the rolling of doubles as already mentioned. 4. Jamie has a great mind and eye for theme, touching on darker aspects of humanity with lightness and humour.

Final thoughts: As it stands however, the incorporation of the roll-a-double mechanic is close to game-breaking, as it introduces a very powerful, yet undesirable luck element. The mechanic was clearly built into the game design with purpose though and removing it does prevent 'combos', the exciting rapid/unforeseen building developments, as well as interactions with worker retraction. Either way, something is lost. Secondly, the game feels really 'same-samey' unless you become aligned with the sky/ cloud faction, as the pathways to victory are just too similar. It's a fun game and worth a try and I would happily play from time to time.

RATING: 7
This game is really interesting. The theme is great, and is very well integrated into the mechanics and the artwork and component design. 

The worker placement mechanic of using rolled dice is a fantastic idea. It reminds me a little of The Castles Of Burgundy in that all dice rolls are equally useful, just some are more useful in some ways and others are more useful in other ways.

RATING: 8
Euphoria, a game that asks players to build a better dystopia, offers an uncommon thematic experience. As a mid-level boss in a dystopian society, you must try to keep your workers dumb and happy, a task to which there is no small measure of luck involved. Nevertheless, Euphoria’s willingness to break from the mold of other worker placement games keeps the gameplay fun and interesting. In contrast to most games where players use workers to effectively block others from taking actions and gaining perks, here anyone can gain the full benefits afforded locations on the board by bumping the current player’s worker back to their owner, the catch here being that this could end up giving that player a free turn on the rebound. Euphoria truly attempts to break new ground in the worker placement genre, and succeeds at doing so while also providing an enjoyable and original experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Euphoria is a worker placement game in which you use dice for workers.  The value of the dice is not that relevant to gameplay--the main reason for dice is that if the total value of your dice ever exceeds a certain value, your workers are "too smart" and you lose one of them (this isn't too hard to avoid).  The remarkable aspect of this game is its use of "micro-actions"--during your turn, you're either placing one worker and doing the action, or pulling your workers back.  There are no rounds, resolution phases, etc.; everything is encompassed in these micro-actions, which makes the game flow really well.  Aside from that, it's a resource management game (the bits are gorgeous) where you're trying to be the first one to 10 VPs.

(3 plays)

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Euphoria is a game set in a dystopian future, wherein each player is… someone trying to do… something. Frankly, I have no idea. For a game with a cool theme, it ends up coming off as pretty abstract. Mechanically, Euphoria is a worker placement game that uses dice as workers. Similar to games such as Tzolkin and Manhattan Project, on a single turn, players either place workers to take actions or pull workers from the board. Each player starts with 10 star token that represent victory points, and the first play to place all of her stars on the board wins immediately. 

There are certainly some interesting mechanisms in the game. Players have to manage a number of resource tracks, one of which is worker intelligence. When workers are pulled from or bumped off of the board, the player rolls all of her dice. If the sum of the dice and the intelligence track exceeds a certain threshold, the workers have become too intelligent and one runs away, meaning the player loses a worker. There are also role cards, some open and some hidden, that grant players special abilities at certain points in the game. Finally, one of the ways to score points involves building markets, which are a set of connected spaces on the board, each of which requires a specific resource. Because there are more spaces than a player typically has workers, once one player starts construction of a market, there is a rush among other players to be contribute to the process. Not only does this provide those players with a point, but after the market is built, a tile flips which has a penalty on it. Those players that contributed to the construction of the market are unaffected by the penalty, so there is additional incentive even beyond the point that construction provides.

There is a lot going on in this game, but at first glance it seems to me that the Hodge-podge of mechanisms obfuscates an otherwise pretty shallow and random game. There is so much hidden information and so many resources to track that it is difficult to see a path to points. Granted, it was my first play and we played with a full complement of six (which I wouldn’t recommend), but there wasn’t enough here to make me want to go back to the game. Euphoria also suffers from some pretty bad art and graphic design, which certainly doesn’t help its case. After hearing so many positive things, this was a big let-down for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Another worker placement game with a blend of resource management, which you convert into achieving a goal. First one to deploy 10 goal stars wins. 
Most of your actions are gathering resources, advancing a power of 1 of the 4 factions, using resources to deploy a goal. There are several hooks.
You start with 2 faction cards one hidden so there a bit of hidden role aspect. 
The markets require some cooperative element and if you ignore it there penalty that hurts enough to force you to play catch up in another method.
The workers a dice so if you have too many available workers you run a risk that you get too high value so your workers become "too smart" and you lose one. 

The came flows quickly since everything is a single action - placing a worker doing the action, bringing workers back, hoping that the others will displace your workers so you don't have to burn a turn to bring them home. Not as sure about the helper cards since they seem to run the gamut from extraordinary to ho-hum, not bad for variety but hard on players with different experience levels.

RATING: 8
First things first, this is a gorgeous game. I was already sold on Euphoria on aesthetics alone. So, does Euphoria have the game mechanics to support such impressive style? Well, that depends...

Everything in Euphoria is synchronized. This is a game where it is very easy to help another player, but pretty difficult to hurt anyone. Once everyone is experienced with the game, you'll discover subtle ways to slow down a winner, but winning will always require a bit of teamwork.

Overall, I love the way the board evolves throughout the game and the secret faction characters always shake things up. Euphoria is basically the definition of semi-cooperative, and even though the game sometimes forces me to be nicer to my opponents than I'd like, I always have a good time with it.

RATING: 8.5
This is a race game to assert your authority over  the world.  Surprisingly it is a really fun euro game that doesn't have a ton of assertive conflict. It's really a race game to get rid of your authority token first.  This is done using worker placement.  Workers are dice and the number rolled can play a factor with placement but it also uses a unique method using the total die rolls to do an awareness check.  The theme integrate great as you feel like you are a mid level authority in a dystopian world trying to keep your workers productive and unaware of their controlled atmosphere.  love the asymmetry  of the starting set up and I can see the replay ability being high as starting ability will be different almost every time.  Just a great unique game with a great theme.

RATING: 7
I backed this on kickstarter (along with many many others), and was incredibly excited about the game - the theme was cool and seemed well-linked to the mechanics, which had several features which I haven't seen before (the building of markets which give penalties to the players not involved in them, the management of knowledge to try to avoid losing workers, the various allegiance bonuses), and a very wide selection of recruit cards with special abilities, to make each game different.

However, inevitably, the "shiny" factor of a new game has worn off by now, and this is no longer one of my top choices - hence I have revised my BGG rating down to a 7. It's a good game, but not outstanding, and there are many other games - including older ones as well as newer - which I would now rather play.

RATING: 8.9
Very rich and fun worker placement resource management game with lots of options and minimum downtime due to clever 'one action' per player/per turn system. Rules are not overly complicated, game is accessible to hardcore as well as to entry players. But you need a few plays to get 'into it'.

And don't be fooled. Game winner is not decided by luck. Even though randomness is present (luckily), player who is more able to constantly adjust to actual situation who can react to opponents move and who could carefully and quickly prepare his "only" action (per turn)  at the end wins...if no such player is present, "lucky" player wins (and still other players could have fun time :-))

Oh...and on top of that....GREAT THEME + GREAT PRODUCTION VALUE, BEAUTIFUL COMPONENTS.

- - - 

Houseruled setup of recruit cards (until expansion comes out):

1. [b]Separate Icarite recruits[/b].

2. Shuffle the deck consisting of [b]all other three faction[/b]s (Euphorian + Subterran + Wastlander) and [b]deal 4 recruits [/b] to each player. 

3. Players [u]select one card[/u] and simultaneously place it face up in front of them [b]showing to others[/b].

4. Then players [u]select second[/u] recruit card [b][COLOR=#990000][b]from remaining 3 cards[/b][/COLOR][/b] and keeps this card hidden from other players.

5. Take [b]all the leftover recruits (including those not chosen by players) and Icarite recruits[/b] and shuffle them together to make the recruit deck for the game.


- - - OPTIONAL RULE:

Trading is not allowed

RATING: 8
This is a really unique strategy game where players are trying to gain influence represented by stars in the different districts of the city. You roll dice to determine your workers intelligence then place those dice on the board to gain resources which help you get influence.  The smarter the worker the more he can produce but if your workers are too smart they realize they are being exploited and leave your workforce, forcing you to use your resources to recruit more.

Pros:Extremely thematic, unique theme, quick gameplay with an element of luck and strategy, unique gameplay mechanism never seen before. 

Cons: games seem too end too quickly.really difficult to teach with many foreign gameplay concepts.

RATING: 6
Apart from the sheer beauty of the components, what separates Euphoria from your average worker placement game is the simplicity, elegance and effectiveness of the dice worker placement system. Where Deadwood and The Manhattan Project used a similar place or remove mechanic, Euphoria has expanded upon the mechanism and structured quite a bit of the game around it. Interesting choices now present themselves as players gamble to keep the number of pips on their dice below 16 to avoid the setback of losing a worker. The reward is having more workers to place before they must be removed again, and an increase in the chances of the wondrous doubles. Watch other players bump a worker off just to push you over the 16 threshold. Watch players camp on the most sought after spaces while removing the rest of their workers. There's some clever choices to make and the dice do a beautiful job of keeping the game interesting.

Unfortunately the rest of the game is a fairly standard resource collection and conversion into victory points exercise dressed in a theme that holds limited appeal for me. It's certainly a decent race into areas and to place those pointy stars, but the game is so forgiving with so many ways to acquire what you need when you need it that there's not much tension, only meticulous planning saved by the fun of betting on the roll of the dice.

Because of the theme the rule book is a bit of a challenge to decipher without the components in front of you, but if you've got someone to explain it to you then you'll find it's a very simple game to pick up.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
This is a race game with worker placement as its central mechanic. Workers either harvest basic resources, turn basic resources into advanced resources, or allow you to pay advanced resources in order to place stars onto a limited number of spaces. Whoever places a fixed number of stars first, wins. 

Since this a race, whoever gets more turns has a distinct advantage. You get more turns than your opponents in one of four different ways, the first two being completely random: (1) by dice rolling multiples of the same number (in effect resulting in your opponent having to skip their turn), (2) by avoiding rolling clutches of very high numbers (resulting in a potential loss of a worker which will cost you 2-3 turns to get back), (3) by having more workers than your opponents, which then minimizes the number of "dead" turns you have to take to get your workers back from the board, and (4) by getting your worker bumped from the board by another player's (or your own) worker when placed in the same spot, again delaying the need to essentially waste a turn to recall your workers back from the board. If the board was tighter, this last mechanic could have resulted in more player interaction, but alas, this is not the case and you are seldom forced to give your opponent an edge by bumping his/her worker.

The game has high quality components and an interesting theme, but for me falls flat in its dry-as-dust game play and high impact of lucky/unlucky dice rolls. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I have only played this game once, but plan to play it again. It's easy to get left behind, especially in a 5 player game.  There really isn't anything extremely new or different in this game; it's mediocre at best and not that much fun.

RATING: N/A
This game was decent and something I wouldn't mind playing now and then.  There is definitely some strategy that you can employ while you play.  I haven't played it enough to feel too comfortable arguing strategy though.

The biggest thing that I disliked was the dice rolling.  When I play a game, the longer it is the less I like chance to be involved.  The dice rolling in this game can be really frustrating.  Especially the rule of getting to place two workers at a time if you roll pairs or higher.

Otherwise the game seems to be one of follow the group.  You want to be involved in doing whatever other people are doing.  What I mean by this is that if you are doing your own thing while everyone else is doing similar actions like build markets, you will probably lose.  This is good and bad.  It is good in that it makes for a bit more interaction and responsive play making.  I dislike it because a decent amount of the game can be decided on what your opponents happened to choose before the game started.

What I mean is the recruitment cards.  Everyone chooses 2 recruit cards from 4 they get at the start.  One is played and the other saved for later.  They all have abilities that help you in the game and a faction.  Besides the fact that I question how balanced these cards are, what everyone chooses decides the pace of the game.  If you chose a recruit card of a faction that no one else went for then you are already behind. (similar to what I said earlier where you want to follow the group)

RATING: 8
I like this game. The theme (or perhaps backstory) is intelligent and fits the mechanics very well. The artwork and components are absolutely gorgeous. The only thing that keeps this from garnering a higher rating is my dislike of the endgame. For the same reason I don't like Settlers of Catan, I yearn for a better endgame here. The whole race to 10 victory points (or in this case, the race to place 10 authority tokens on the board) feels a little... underdeveloped for me. I'd prefer something like every time a player places a token, he or she gets X victory points (maybe decreasing with each placement; or perhaps based on how much moral you currently have; or even the lower of moral or intelligence when placed). Then the first player to place the 10th token only triggers the end of game, and there's still victory points to consider for winner. I don't know; maybe it wouldn't work in practice. It's just a personal taste thing, and I don't like the racing mechanism (at least not like this). Also: even though it says 60 minutes, our games are always much longer. Our last game with 5 player, for instance, lasted 2.5 hours. A little too long for what you get.

Colorful and nice to look at for a cube conversion game!

RATING: 4
Great presentation on this one: the artwork is interesting, the dice are fun to look at yet still functional, and the carved wooden pieces are a nice touch. For a Euro game, the theme is both interesting and actually woven in decently. The interesting idea here is how if the total of your dice is too much and your workers get too smart, you have to return them. I like how the spaces provide more reward the more people are there and how your recruits are uncovered once a bunch of actions have been taken with a single faction.

But why don't I like it? First and foremost, it is a race game, and I'm not super fond of those, although if well done, I've found them enjoyable (see [thing=127023][/thing]). But here I find that the arc is pretty dull here. Primarily, you need to open up new markets in order to be able to place enough stars, and to open up the new markets, you need cooperation to make that happen, or just let your opponents churn through and score a bunch of points through cards. The strategies are not really multi-faceted: you churn through the Icarian faction, as it gets you stars and cards, and then if someone is building a market, then you can just swoop in and get the same star as they did. And don't get me started about introducing trading into the game: unless it's a true negotiation game (which this really isn't), it's a hack to make it easier to facilitate the cooperation that a better design would have generated. Such potential in this and unfortunately wasted.

RATING: 7
1 play, 5er

Pretty interesting worker-placement game. We learned it more from a player aid than the actual rules, so the theme really didn't come through at all (except for how having too much knowledge makes your workers likely to leave you!). Will need to try it again, with people who know the rules.

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
This is a worker placement game where dice are the workers. The (VERY busy) board shows 3 cities, each with a tunnel track and 2 face down (randomly chosen from a large set) market tiles; and a 4th city with several face up markets -- each city is associated with a faction. There are also several action spaces, a track for knowledge and morale (1-6 each), and a track for each of the 4 factions in the game. Players get 4 character cards, keeping one face up and another face down; these belong to 1 of the 4 factions and give you an extra power when face up.

On your turn, either ...
- retrieve any/all dice (either pay a resource and gain 2 morale, or pay nothing and lose 1 morale)
OR
- place one die; if you have matched dice, may take multiple turns placing them one by one
Any time you get any dice back, roll it/them, add to any pips already in front of you, and add your knowledge track position. If sum >15, return your highest die to Supply.

Placing dice may require payment of resources, and can do a variety of things:
- acquire resources (several types; get more with higher sums)
- allow contributing towards building (and flipping) a market
- gain a die (max 4)
- advance tunnel track (get far enough and that faction's influence track advances; get to the end an extra resource-acquiring action space opens for players supporting the faction)
- build a city piece
- may also advance a faction's influence track; as these advance, they cross thresholds giving players with that faction's character card face up additional bonuses (e.g., acquire more resources, flip face down character cards)

Players have 10 authority tokens. These are placed when building city pieces, building markets, or with some character cards. The first player to place them all wins!

RATING: 10

RATING: 8.5
2-6, 60m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is an excellent, very fresh feeling, worker placement game.  The goal of Euphoria is to collect enough resources to place "stars" in certain areas on the board.  The places that can accommodate stars are limited depending on the number of players so there is a bit of a race to get to these as well.

Collecting the resources is somewhat standard worker placement fare, just place one of your workers on a space and collect the printed amount of materials.  However the unique thing here is that we are trying to keep our workers stupid and happy because if they get too smart we lose them.  The workers in this game are dice and the pips showing on the dice may grant us more resources but they also represent the worker's intelligence.  This also means that while like most worker placement games, more workers means more actions which means better chance to win they also get more intelligent in numbers and you run a higher risk of losing one.  This balance makes for a lot of interesting decisions.

The other thing I really like about the game play is that each player has a unique personality that gives a minor bonus revealed at the start of the game and another that gets revealed later.  These personalities belong to one of four factions that advance and grant more bonuses to players if they have a personality belonging to that faction later in the game.  So revealing these second personalities later in the game can twist things up, especially if now you are taking advantage of bonuses you allowed an opponent to unlock for you.

Also, even with the non-deluxe version, the components are awesome.  All the resources are different shapes (they look like the marshmallows in a box of Lucky Charms) and the board and cards are brightly colored with wonderful artwork.  I really enjoy this game, yet another one I'm sad I missed the Kickstarter boat for.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6

Only half the resources are relevant to you in any given game, and you can get punished for not being lucky enough to select members of the same factions that other players have selected. Most of the special powers are marginal/situational so they don't have a whole lot impact on the game. It's a lot of little things that I've noticed over time that just kind of build up. Also there's a trading rule for some reason? I'm not sure if it improves the game or not because nobody wants to do it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I like the art, the theme, and the components (though the board might be a bit too busy,) but I think there are some clashing combinations here that just don't suit me.  It's a race game, which is fine, but efficiency is king here, and gaining what are essentially extra actions just through rolling well (doubles/not losing workers) or drawing the right cards feels excessively luck oriented to me.  

Also, I think the early game might just be a little too rough for the kind of game this is.  I like the market mechanic, but losing efficiency early to someone who is rolling better could mean falling hopeless far behind, as happened to one player in our game.  There were just too many punitive markets for him to be able to do anything in the 2nd half of the game. Playing out the 2nd half of a 90 minute game with very little chance to win is not a good time, and while that can happen in many board games, it is especially painful in this one.  I suspect that doubles is in there partially as a comeback mechanic for people who are really far behind, but it can just as easily both create and exacerbate the problem.

I'd call it an interesting misfire, but I wouldn't be especially excited to play it again.

RATING: 7
tl;dr: quite nice

Here's another game that recycles a lot of well known elements: gathering resources, worker placement blahblahblah... and while it isn't exceptional, it works quite well. There are some nice twists with the way the markets come in to play and the bumping of player pieces. It doesn't really do anything new, but it's enjoyable enough and the weight to length ratio is pretty good.
A small point on the bits: I'm not a fan of the design on the board. It makes the nice artwork look ugly. And did they really need to spend extra money on making custom bits for the hearts and the heads? It's nice of course, but it's also complete overkill. In this case a lower price point would have been a far better option that souping up the bits.
If this is the kind of game we can continue to expect from Stonemaier games, I'll be very happy to play their future offerings.

Score: 7/10
BGG scale: 7/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Just deeply uninspiring. I quite liked how the numbers you rolled were almost entirely irrelevant; as though it was a dice game designed by someone who feels that they have terrible luck with dice and so minimised the effect it would have and then, where it does make a difference, you want to roll low numbers. But in that case, why bother using dice at all? (Oh, that's right, it was on Kickstarter and so the custom dice were a selling point).

In our play, one player lost a die on turn 3, with 2 dice in play and knowledge adding 4 to his roll, he rolled a 12 which cost him a worker and basically eliminated him from play. Half of the table took workers who were Subterrans or the cloud people, meaning that the 2 who didn't were left behind as the others co-operated to flip their hidden characters and place points quickly. The one guy whose early ability seemed massively overpowered won and I came a relatively close second by spamming the same three actions for over 80% of my turns (one of those actions was to retrieve my dice every three turns). That does not result in a fun play.

As for the resource tokens; they're pretty if more than a little garish and I'd be perfectly happy just using coloured cubes. The spreadsheet track to multiply your resources is a (clever) fudge for the fact that the pimped resources were clearly too expensive and so they didn't supply enough.

Final thought: I mostly dislike Kickstarter games for their lack of development. I'm shocked (shocked!) to discover that the rules changed after release; the cost of a morale to place doubles in one turn really should have been in the ruleset from day one. Kickstarter publishers: your customers are not your play testers!

RATING: 8.5
1. The Deluxe version is truly deluxe. The pieces are amazing.
2. I'm in the game - I discovered a boardgame artifact!

This is a worker placement game. Now, I'm normally pretty middle of the road with this mechanic, but I really enjoy this game. The variable markets and recruits means that every game will need a little different approach (though not so much as to make your approach feel like a different game each time). The workers as a die approach is fun as it means there is a little press your luck (or manage your luck I guess) in that you can lose workers if the dice totals are too high. Like most euros, the theme is not everything, but it makes a little more sense than most and is fun.

Be warned. The game will be going along and then suddenly over. For me, most Stonemaier games are like this, so the pacing of the games takes a play to understand well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I enjoyed playing Euphoria, but I'm not really sure how much I'd enjoy playing it again. There is a lot of interesting stuff here, but too much random.

You're playing a worker placement game where you use dice to claim spots. But whenever you take workers back home you have to roll them and if the sum of all your dice and your intelligence rating exceeds 16 then your smartest idle worker wises up and runs away. The goal of the game is to get all your stars placed on the board first. There are several ways to do that, which mostly involve gathering resources or cards.

My problem with the game is that this thing is just chuck full of randomness. At the beginning of the game you get four special power cards and keep two. One is visible and active and the other is hidden. Some of these cards are just flat out better than others. And if you're lucky enough to get two of the same color, then you can push that color track to get a star on both cards which is huge. In my game myself and one other person both had two cards of the same color. The other two players didn't. Additionally our colors were the same as each other. Which meant we both had a vested interest in pushing that color as hard as possible. That was a huge advantage for us.

Additionally when you place a die in certain spots and someone else takes that spot, you get your die back without having to take a retrieve action. Which meant that while Dan and I were pushing up the Yellow track, we kept bumping each other off the same spot... which meant we had to take fewer retrieve actions than the other players.

Placing stars in a lot of locations required three cards or two matching cards. I got a lot of matching pairs which other players didn't. There was one spot that required a certain card, which I got but other people didn't. Lucking into the right cards can help a lot

When you roll doubles, triples, etc. you get to place all the matching dice, while everyone else has to place one at a time. The second place player rolled doubles seven or eight times whereas other players did it one or twice.

Speaking of special powers, my main power was one that let me pay gold when bumping people out of spots and get cards... which let me build a card engine which was terribly powerful. Another player could sacrifice a die to place two stars at once. Those powers blew away the other two player's powers.

It felt like there was more random in the game than there was not-random. While it was fun and enjoyable, I can't see myself enjoying this as much if luck wasn't going my way. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
Struck me as JASE, but with some weird and rather minor randomness. (That's "minor" from a game design standpoint, as in, you could probably remove it. Having your third worker die because you rolled bad is annoying in a less minor way.)

It's a worker placement game (with lots of placement options and the feeling of there being no "big moves") and also a race game. The placement part of the game feels loose (little sense of constraint or interaction) so the tension seemingly must come from the racing component.

The theme is bizarre, which is fine, but the integration into the mechanics is weak and doesn't help you learn the game. E.g. you have a frequently adjusted "morale" score whose main purpose is to allow you to hold more artifacts (stuff like teddy bears and baseball bats) at a time (so, hand size). Morale goes either up or down when you retrieve your workers, based on whether you pay a resource.

Also you roll dice with gears on them to determine how intelligent your workers are each time you deploy them, and if you roll too high you lose a worker. I had no idea that any of this was what was (thematically) happening until after the game when I had a chance to read about it. Really bad.

One thing I did like is that players can cooperatively choose to build new worker placement spots, and only those who contributed to its construction can use it. And further, until the other players pay some extra cost, there's a game-wide negative effect on them. This is a nice kind of player interaction.

Less pleasing are the tracks that can cooperatively be advanced, as whether you can benefit, and whether anyone is going to be helping you advance your tracks of choice, is largely based on two cards you picked at the beginning of the game. (Those cards also have annoying special powers.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 1
I entered into the world of Euphoria extremely excited. It has the most amazing components I have ever seen and with the promise (from others) of exquisite game play, the hype was there. I was ready. 

What I found was a serious mess. This is a race game where all the players are on unequal starting positions. This would be all fine and dandy if as a group we could collectively peg the leader back. Unfortunately, even though it's a "worker" placement game, there's no blocking in this game. Not only this, but if you roll doubles, then you get to take extra turns. If you roll extremely low, you're improving your chances as well. 

The game made so little thematic sense that when people talk about pasted on theme, I think of this game. Workers are shown by their intelligence, denoted by the pips on the die. I don't know how they don't get smarter the longer they experience the real world, nor do I understand why you have to re-roll their intelligence once you recall your workers.

I left Euphoria disappointed. Disappointed and unwilling to return.

Update: I read that the cards are getting a re-balance. Props to the designer on that, but it does make you wonder why this wasn't developed and tested properly in the first place.

RATING: 5
As much as I've wanted to enjoy this game I'm sad to say it just didn't jive with m e.  For reasons I've been trying to pinpoint.  Think it's a combination of there not feeling like dice rolls carry enough weight to them, while at the same time the luck in the game can be frustrating.  This is coming from someone who enjoys randomness in games.  There's too many finicky little elements to worry about that don't really feel cohesive in the game.  

Roll your dice, check to make sure you're under the knowledge track total, can't go there because your numbers are too high, guess you'll go here instead, don't forget that you get a bonus there, but only if you get your worker card flipped, oh and don't forget the obscure ability he grants you, or the negative effects on buildings you didn't participate in.  Get one resource, turn it into another resource, trade that resource in to build a building.

Overall it just felt tedious and un-exciting and more like a chore to setup, play and put away.

RATING: 7
Theme is great.  Names on the cards are awesome, and the building structures and various resources make sense given the theme.  Though this is worker placement, here it actually makes some amount of sense, rather than feeling like an abstract mechanic glued on because it's trendy.  Euro mechanics that turn commodities into resources are dry and boring in a normal game, but that feeling is great for a industrialized dystopian future where the goal is a repeatable process: input + labor = output.

It's more tightly balanced than most other Kickstarter games I've played.  Some ways of obtaining points are easier, but those are generally open to all of the players equally.  The uniqueness of making workers dice and having to risk losing a worker in order to operate at maximum efficiency is great.  There's also interaction in trying to predict where you can get your workers back from where other people will want to bump them off.  

Unfortunately, the game can be fragile.  High die rolls may be unlikely, but they're crippling if they happen.  In addition, the markets that come out can completely wipe out your strategy if you don't get on the right ones, which is both completely unpredictable and nearly uncontrollable.  If players are prepared to roll with the punches, this can be assuaged.

RATING: 8
First Impression: This is a worker placement game where colored dice play the role of the workers. While the core concept of what one does on his turn is extremely simple, there is a ton of possible actions and paths for one to consider. In fact, if it wasn't for the superb production and graphic depection of all the actions one could be overwhelmed by the display of information. An excellent worker placement game with straight forward goals (place ten stars) which requires deep thinking. Certainly not an entry level game, but one which more experienced euro players should thoroughly enjoy.

RATING: 9.5
Would've been so awesome to be in on the Kickstarter for this one. The designers treated their backers to a deluxe set of tokens and all sorts of extras. I doubled my contribution for another game that may not get as much play time as this one probably will. All these guys asked for was an additional $20 for their fancy tokens.

Anyone curious about this game, I highly recommend you watch the first 10-15 minutes of Rahdo's gameplay runthrough (part 1).

** This game is so easy to learn. The board layout is so intuitive. Its design has been very well thought out. The designers of Viticulture certainly have their work cut out for them! I scored it 8/10 and that's with just me play testing by myself to learn it. I'm bringing this to my next "Meaty Gaming" event, for sure!

*** Turns out, this was a fast favorite at my FLGS. It's great because it supports up to 6 players and interaction between players is high. It's really a great and balanced game that encourages competitive play while remaining light enough to laugh and really enjoy every aspect of the game.

RATING: 8.225
I got the Kickstarter Supreme Edition and I think this game is beautiful. The components and especially the resources are amazing. If you missed the Kickstarter, but you want the beautiful ressources, then you can order very, very similar ones now on the Stonemaier Games website. 

The gameplay mechanics are great. A dice-worker placement game where you can't be blocked to go on a field, but sometimes there's an advantage on being first, because then you can get bumped out and use that worker again.

The rules are quite easy to learn (especially when I compare it to other games I play with my gaming group), but when you play it the first time the choices where you want to place your workers can be a bit overwhelming. 

Conclusion: I would definitely recommend it.

RATING: 5
First impression: I really wasn't sure enough how I felt about this to rate it, but I want to capture my initial thoughts about it.  It has some very interesting mechanisms that (for me) didn't gel into an interesting game.
Strike 1: The dystopian theme puts me off, which I could ignore if the rest of the game is worth it.
Strike 2: The way the dice actually worked didn't thrill me.
Strike 3: The engine building seems like it will give good replay value, but can produce very lopsided play experiences.  In the long run, I'm sure this is a great feature if you really dig the game.

This sounds pretty damning, but the respectable elements of the game keep it in an "I'd play it another time or two" zone.  With luck, I'd end up really digging it.
